Section 2 · From Bone‑on‑Nerve to Tone‑First — An Historical Evolution
2.0 Why Revisit Chiropractic History?
“What if the real mechanism of subluxation has been hiding in plain sight?”

Since 1895 the chiropractic story has revolved around the vertebral subluxation as a bone‑on‑nerve problem. That model produced life‑changing results, but it also obscured the deeper insight that tone precedes bone. Tracing the profession’s evolution from compression to tension to tone‑first prepares us to understand the NeuroSpinal paradigm at the heart of Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC).

2.1 The Traditional Subluxation Model—Compression First
Historically, vertebral subluxation was defined as:

A misalignment or fixation of one or more spinal vertebrae

Interfering with nerve transmission via mechanical compression or irritation

Causing downstream dysfunction and dis‑ease in the body

This structural frame drove motion‑palpation, segmental listings, and high‑velocity adjustments—effective tools, yet silent on how stress adaptation (the body’s moment‑to‑moment capacity to integrate physical, chemical, and emotional input) and neurological tone influence health.

2.2 The Compression Myth and Tension‑Based Insight
Key Figure / Study	Core Finding	Implication
Alf Breig (1978)	Adverse Mechanical Tension (AMT) in meninges can alter neural function without vertebral displacement.	Compression isn’t required for interference—tension matters.
Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy (2007)	Altered sensorimotor integration traced to distorted afferent input, not nerve pinch.	Interference is primarily affective and tonal.
Together they dismantled the compression narrative and legitimated a tension‑interference model.

2.3 Foundational Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic
Early innovators sensed that low‑force input could influence global tone:

Era / Technique	Tonal Contribution
Logan Basic (1931)	Low‑force sacral contacts to balance postural tone.
Directional Non‑Force (1940s)	Pressure testing & leg checks as reflex indicators.
Upper Cervical / Grostic (1930s‑80s)	Dentate‑ligament tension hypothesis—segmental misalignment ↔ global dural tone.
Toftness (1950s)	Electromagnetic field detection of interference.
Thompson (1955)	Reflex leg checks later reframed as tone signals.
Network Spinal Analysis (1987)	Wave dynamics that reflect neural re‑organization.
(Expanded notes appear in Appendix A.)

2.4 OsseoTonal Techniques—Blending Structure and Tone
From the mid‑1990s forward, several methods fused articular contacts with tonal analysis and intent. We label these OsseoTonal:

Technique	Distinguishing Features
Torque Release Technique (1995)	Instrument‑assisted input; pressure testing + leg checks; tonal intent.
MC2 (1996)	Tonal balance via upper‑cervical contacts and reflex indicators.
Bio‑Geometric Integration (1995)	Biotensegrity & wave dynamics guide osseous contacts.
Mastery Love Service; Kairos; Syntropy; TIC; Pneuma	Combine precise osseous vectors with tonal presence, breath, or flow‑state principles.
These hybrids confirmed that specific intent and neurological indicators improve outcomes even when correction is delivered through bone.

2.5 Emergence of the NeuroSpinal Model — Talsky Tonal Chiropractic
Recognising that bone contacts still approached tone indirectly, Dr. Marvin Talsky developed Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) in 2001:

Focus: direct engagement with the Cranio‑Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C‑SMFU)

Analysis: tonal pressure testing + leg checks → identify the Best Window In

Delivery: least force, most precise direction of ease parallel to aberrant tension

Verification: immediate reflex change confirms the body, not the practitioner, initiates correction

This clarified that subluxation begins as a global tone distortion; vertebral misalignment is a downstream compensation.

2.6 Key Take‑Aways
Bone‑on‑nerve compression is insufficient to explain modern findings.

Aberrant NeuroSpinal tone is the primary interference; vertebral misalignment is secondary.

The C‑SMFU supplies the missing anatomical‑physiological mechanism linking stress adaptation to structural change.

TTC is the first fully non‑articular protocol to engage that mechanism directly.

Up Next — Section 3: Detailed anatomy and physiology of the C‑SMFU, the fountainhead of tone.

(End Section 2. All citations will be formatted APA 7 in the final reference list.)

You said:
why is this use the word "An" here instead of "A"
An Historical Evolution
ChatGPT said:
“An historical” is an older, chiefly British convention dating to a time when the “h” in historical (and historic) was either silent or only lightly aspirated, making the word begin with a vowel sound. Today, in modern American English the “h” is fully pronounced, so the standard article is “a.”

Recommended change

Section 2 · From Bone‑on‑Nerve to Tone‑First — A Historical Evolution

Using “A historical” will feel natural to most readers (especially a North‑American audience) and still satisfies formal style guides such as Chicago, APA, and AMA.

This continuous, integrative system comprises the brain, spinal cord, multi‑layered meninges, cerebrospinal fluid, and the dural attachments that tether these tissues throughout the cranium and spine. When physical, chemical, or emotional stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU contracts. That contraction generates aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, throttling sensory bandwidth, limiting adaptability, and fragmenting the body’s coherence—often preceding any visible bone misalignment  (Breig  1978; Ward  1980).

Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) builds on foundational chiropractic theory, decades of hands‑on experience, and modern neuroscience. TTC recognizes vertebral subluxation not as the primary interference but as a compensatory adaptation to global neurological tension. Correction is achieved not through high‑force mobilization of fixations, but through information‑rich input delivered at a precise, non‑articular contact aligned with the direction of ease—the vector parallel to the aberrant tension within the C‑SMFU.

Supported by research from Breig  (1978), Ward  (1980), Haavik  (2007), Oschman  (2000), Becker  &  Seldon  (1985), and others, TTC reframes the chiropractic adjustment as a neurological communication—a touch‑based dialogue, delivered with congruent intent, that facilitates the body to reinitiate the process of self‑adjusting, correcting, and returning to wholeness.

Executive Summary
“Subluxation is always global, and the primary is always NeuroSpinal.”

The science of chiropractic is evolving—and so must our understanding of subluxation. For more than a century the profession focused on the vertebral subluxation: bones misaligned or fixated, compressing nerves and disrupting function. While that bone‑first lens provided a structural foundation, modern anatomy, neurophysiology, and decades of clinical practice now reveal a far more global phenomenon.

This white paper substantiates a paradigm shift: subluxation is fundamentally a state of aberrant tone in the NeuroSpinal System—also known as the Cranio‑Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C‑SMFU). This continuous, integrative system comprises the brain, spinal cord, multi‑layered meninges, cerebrospinal fluid, and the dural attachments that tether these tissues throughout the cranium and spine. When physical, chemical, or emotional stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU contracts. That contraction generates aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, throttling sensory bandwidth, limiting adaptability, and fragmenting the body’s coherence—often preceding any visible bone misalignment (Breig 1978; Ward 1980).

Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) builds on foundational chiropractic theory, decades of hands‑on experience, and modern neuroscience. TTC recognizes vertebral subluxation not as the primary interference but as a compensatory adaptation to global neurological tension. Correction is achieved not through high‑force mobilization of fixations, but through information‑rich input delivered at a precise, non‑articular contact aligned with the direction of ease—the vector parallel to the aberrant tension within the C‑SMFU.

Supported by research from Breig (1978), Ward (1980), Haavik (2007), Oschman (2000), Becker & Seldon (1985), and others, TTC reframes the chiropractic adjustment as a neurological communication—a touch‑based dialogue, delivered with congruent intent, that facilitates the body to reinitiate the process of self‑adjusting, correcting, and returning to wholeness.

This paper will:

Trace the historical evolution of tonal chiropractic thought.

Present the anatomical and physiological basis of the C‑SMFU.

Examine the distinction between OsseoTonal and direct tonal engagement.

Explore information‑driven pathways through which the body organizes, adapts, and corrects.

Clarify how TTC integrates with and enhances existing chiropractic techniques.

In doing so, we offer a lens for the future of principled chiropractic—precise, neurologically grounded, and built on the body’s innate capacity for continuous self‑optimization.

2 · From Bone‑on‑Nerve to Tone‑First — A Historical Evolution
2.0 Opening Perspective
“What if the real mechanism of subluxation has been hiding in plain sight?”

The concept of subluxation has anchored chiropractic from its inception. In early practice it was framed almost exclusively in structural terms: a vertebra misaligned, compressing a spinal nerve, and disrupting the expression of Innate Intelligence. This bone‑on‑nerve model became the cornerstone of 20th‑century chiropractic philosophy and technique.

“Life is an expression of tone; the cause of disease is any variation in tone.”
— D. D. Palmer, The Chiropractor’s Adjuster (1910)

Even in Palmer’s day, some suspected that subluxation was more than mechanical fixation. Decade by decade, pioneers observed that nervous‑system tone, coherence, and adaptability are primary indicators of health. They proposed that subluxation might be tonal first, structural second—setting the stage for today’s NeuroSpinal paradigm.

(The sections that follow trace this progression from bone‑on‑nerve compression to tension interference and, ultimately, to the tone‑first model embodied in Talsky Tonal Chiropractic.)

2.1 The Traditional Subluxation Model—Compression First
For most of chiropractic history, the vertebral subluxation has been defined as:

A misalignment or fixation of one or more spinal vertebrae

Interfering with nerve transmission via mechanical compression or irritation

Causing downstream dysfunction and dis‑ease in the body

This bone‑centric definition inspired motion‑palpation, segmental X‑ray listings, thermographic scans, and high‑velocity adjustments—clinical methods that still help millions today. Yet the classic model leaves two critical questions unanswered:

Stress adaptation. Why does a particular vertebra fixate in the first place? The compression narrative offers no mechanism linking physical, chemical, or emotional stress to the moment a joint becomes “stuck.”

Neurological tone. How do global shifts in muscular and meningeal tension influence perception, motor control, and healing? The model treats tone changes as effects, not potential causes.

These gaps set the stage for the tension‑interference discoveries that reshaped chiropractic thinking in the late 20th century—leading from compression to tension and, ultimately, to tone‑first paradigms.

2.2 The Compression Myth and the Rise of Tension‑Based Insight
For decades chiropractic textbooks taught that a displaced vertebra “pinches” a spinal nerve. Yet careful investigation has shown that outright compression is rare outside gross trauma or tumor. Two milestone research programs turned the spotlight from pinch to tension.

Research program	Core discovery	Take‑away
Alf Breig, M.D. – “Adverse Mechanical Tension” (1978)	Excess stretch or slack in the meninges and cord can distort neural firing without vertebral displacement.	Mechanical tension is sufficient to interfere with nerve physiology.
Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy – somatosensory evoked potentials (2000s)	Cortical processing changes follow altered afferent input and meningeal tension, not nerve pinch.	Interference is primarily afferent distortion, rooted in tone.
Allostatic load, not pathology
When incoming stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU tightens as an allostatic response—reducing range of motion so less sensory data floods the system (§ 3.2 b). This protective tension is not pathological at first, but it distorts proprioceptive signaling:

Misinformation: skewed data from hyper‑toned tissues

Missing information: silenced pathways from restricted motion

If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. If it persists, the protective pattern becomes maladaptive, laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathology. Thus, interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—reshaping the profession’s focus from bones to tone.

You said:
does proprioceptive signaling cover all communication to and from brain and body
ChatGPT said:
No—proprioceptive signaling is only one slice of the brain‑body information highway. It refers specifically to afferent input about position, movement, and load from muscles, joints, and fascia. Full two‑way communication includes:

Stream	Examples	Direction
Proprioception	Muscle spindles, Golgi tendon organs, joint mechanoreceptors	Body ➜ Brain
Interoception	Visceral stretch, chemo‑receptors, baro‑receptors	Body ➜ Brain
Exteroception	Touch, temperature, nociception, vision, audition	Environment/Body ➜ Brain
Efferent Motor	Alpha/gamma motor neurons, corticospinal tracts	Brain ➜ Muscles
Autonomic Efferent	Sympathetic & parasympathetic outputs	Brain/Brainstem ➜ Organs
Neuroendocrine	HPA‑axis hormones, cytokine signaling	Brain ➜ Body (via bloodstream)
So if you want to cover all communication distorted by aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, use a broader phrase such as “afferent and efferent signaling,” “sensorimotor and autonomic signaling,” or simply “neurological signaling between brain and body.”

Revision for § 2.2 line

“This protective tension distorts afferent and efferent signaling—skewing proprioceptive, interoceptive, and autonomic messages alike.”

2.2 The Compression Myth and the Rise of Tension‑Based Insight
For decades chiropractic textbooks taught that a displaced vertebra “pinches” a spinal nerve. Yet careful investigation has shown that outright compression is rare outside gross trauma or tumor. Two milestone research programs turned the spotlight from pinch to tension.

Research program	Core discovery	Take‑away
Alf Breig, M.D. – “Adverse Mechanical Tension” (1978)	Excess stretch or slack in the meninges and cord can distort neural firing without vertebral displacement.	Mechanical tension is sufficient to interfere with nerve physiology.

You said:
how do the menengies communicate witht teh CNS
ChatGPT said:
The meninges “talk” to the central nervous system (CNS) through several overlapping channels—mechanical, neurochemical, vascular, and even electrical. Here’s how the three meningeal layers (dura mater, arachnoid mater, pia mater) stay in constant dialogue with the brain and spinal cord they envelop:

Communication pathway	How it works	What it influences
Mechanical coupling	Head movement, posture changes, or trauma create stretch/compression forces in the dura. Tether points (e.g., falx, tentorium, dentate ligaments, filum terminale) transmit those forces directly to the underlying neural tissue.	Neuronal firing thresholds, proprioceptive feedback, and CSF dynamics.
Cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) pulse signaling	Arterial pulsations and respiration drive CSF waveforms through the sub‑arachnoid space. Pressure changes deform perivascular astrocyte end‑feet and piezo‑sensitive ion channels.	Waste clearance (glymphatic flow), intracranial pressure regulation, and mechano‑transduction to neurons/glia.
Durally embedded nerve endings	The dura is richly innervated by trigeminal and cervical sensory fibers plus sympathetic efferents. Stretch or chemical irritation fires those fibers → brainstem and thalamic nuclei.	Headache pain, autonomic set‑points, and nociceptive modulation.
Meningeal immune surveillance	Meningeal lymphatic vessels drain antigens to cervical nodes; resident macrophages and mast cells release cytokines that diffuse into superficial cortex.	Neuro‑immune cross‑talk; neuroinflammation or repair signaling.
Vascular/chemical signaling	Dural arteries and veins release nitric oxide, ATP, prostaglandins; vasoactive compounds permeate perivascular spaces reaching astrocytes.	Cerebral blood‑flow regulation, neurovascular coupling.
Electromechanical conduction in collagen	Collagen in the dura displays piezoelectricity; deformation generates charge that can propagate along fibrils (“living matrix” hypothesis).	Rapid analog signaling that may modulate neuronal membrane potential, though still under investigation.
Key take‑aways
It’s bidirectional. The CNS doesn’t just sit inside a sack; neural activity affects vascular tone and CSF pulsatility, which in turn modify meningeal tension.

Tone translates to signaling. When the dura tightens (e.g., in an allostatic response), it changes mechanical input to both nerve endings and glial mechanosensors, altering afferent traffic to the brain.

Chemical chatter matters. Cytokines, neuropeptides, and nitric oxide released within the meningeal layers can diffuse a few hundred microns—enough to reach cortical and spinal neurons.

Clinical implication. Techniques that reduce adverse meningeal tension (e.g., certain cranial, tonal, or NeuroSpinal contacts) may improve neural signaling by normalizing these mechanical‑chemical pathways.

So the meninges are not passive wrappers; they’re an active, sensing, signaling interface—one reason the NeuroSpinal (C‑SMFU) model treats meningeal tone as a primary regulator of neural function.

You said:
So menegial system gets overwelmebed and increases tension, what pathways are used to send signals to the CNS? is dorssal horn involved
ChatGPT said:
How meningeal “over‑tension” reaches the CNS
When the cranio‑spinal meninges tighten under overload, at least three afferent pathways carry that information into the central nervous system. Yes—the dorsal horn of the spinal cord (and its cranial analogue, the spinal trigeminal nucleus) is one of the key relay sites.

Pathway	Where the fibers run	First central synapse	Signal type & relevance
Spinal meningeal (dural) afferents from C1–C3	Small‑diameter A‑δ and C fibers leave the dura via the sinuvertebral nerve or posterior meningeal branches → dorsal‑root ganglia of C1–C3	Dorsal horn lamina I & V (upper cervical cord)	Nociceptive & mechano‑sensitive firing encodes stretch, chemical irritation, pH shifts. Contributes to cervico‑genic headache and reflex muscle splinting down the spine.
Trigeminal meningeal afferents (V1/V2 branches)	Dural fibers travel with the ophthalmic & maxillary divisions of CN V → trigeminal (Gasserian) ganglion	Spinal trigeminal nucleus caudalis (medulla–C2 interface)	Same class of A‑δ/C mechanosensors; major route for cranial dural tension to influence brainstem autonomic centers and cortical pain maps.
Perivascular & paravascular stretch sensors	Piezo‑sensitive endings on meningeal arteries/veins and along CSF pathways; some terminate on astrocyte end‑feet rather than neurons	Astrocyte–neuronal tripartite synapses in superficial cortex & dorsal horn	Transduce pulsation and tension into ATP / glutamate release, modulating local neuronal excitability (“volume control knob”).
Chemical back‑channels

Mast cells & macrophages in the dura release histamine, cytokines, and prostaglandins when stretched or stressed.

These mediators diffuse a few hundred microns—enough to influence superficial cortical neurons and dorsal‑horn wide‑dynamic‑range (WDR) cells.

Outcome inside the dorsal horn / trigeminal nucleus

Increased afferent firing → WDR neurons ramp up background activity.

Central sensitization → lower thresholds for spinal reflexes and pain perception.

Efferent reflex loops (via γ‑ and α‑motor neurons) tighten paraspinal musculature, further stabilizing the tense dural sleeve—closing the feedback loop.

Bottom line

Yes—the dorsal horn is deeply involved. Meningeal tension is encoded by:

Dural A‑δ/C fibers → dorsal‑horn laminae I & V (spinal)

Dural trigeminal fibers → spinal trigeminal nucleus (cranial)

From there the signals ascend via spinothalamic, spinoreticular, and trigeminothalamic tracts, and descend via segmental reflex loops—altering posture, autonomic tone, and pain perception. This is how an overloaded, tightened meningeal system “tells” the CNS it has reached its adaptive limit.

what gts tignal from increase tension on CSMFU to dorsal horn

How “extra tension” in the C‑SMFU reaches the dorsal horn
(the nuts‑and‑bolts pathway)

Step	What happens anatomically	Key players
1 · Dural stretch / shear	The contracting C‑SMFU elongates or shears the spinal dura and its attachments (filum terminale, dentate ligaments, periosteal anchors).	Collagen + embedded free nerve endings
2 · Mechanotransduction in dural nerve endings	Stretch‑gated ion channels (Piezo‑1/2, ASICs, TRPV4) on unmyelinated C‑fibers and thinly myelinated A‑δ fibers depolarize.	Meningeal afferents (group III & IV)
3 · Peripheral conduction to the dorsal‑root ganglion (DRG)	Those afferent fibers travel in the recurrent meningeal (sinuvertebral) nerve or small posterior dural branches, then pass through the IVF to the DRG—especially at C1–C3 and each lower segment.	DRG Soma of dural afferent neuron
4 · Central entry into spinal cord	The central branch of the same neuron enters the cord via the dorsal root and terminates primarily in lamina I, outer lamina II, and lamina V.	Dorsal horn projection neurons (incl. wide‑dynamic‑range cells)
5 · Synaptic transmission	Neurotransmitters: glutamate, substance P, CGRP; postsynaptic depolarization of nociceptive & propriospinal interneurons.	NMDA/AMPA receptors, NK‑1
6 · Ascending & local effects	- Ascending: spinothalamic & spinoreticular tracts relay “meningeal tension” to thalamus / limbic / cortical areas.
- Local: segmental reflex arcs via γ‑ and α‑motor neurons increase paraspinal tone, reinforcing splinting.	Central sensitization + muscle co‑contraction
Quick mnemonic
“S‑P‑D‑C‑A” – Stretch ▶ Piezo ▶ DRG ▶ Cord ▶ Ascend/Adapt

Why C1–C3 are special
The upper cervical dura is richly innervated, and its afferents converge in the trigeminocervical complex (spinal trigeminal nucleus + C1–C3 dorsal horn). That convergence explains:

Cervicogenic headaches referred from dural tension

Neck muscle guarding when cranial meninges tighten

Chemical co‑messengers
Stretch also triggers mast‑cell degranulation inside the dura → histamine, prostaglandins, and ATP further sensitize nearby afferents, amplifying the dorsal‑horn signal.

Bottom line
The “wiring” from increased C‑SMFU tension to the dorsal horn is a direct meningeal afferent → DRG → lamina I/II/V pathway. Once that path fires long enough, dorsal‑horn neurons become sensitized, autonomic tone shifts, paraspinal muscles contract—and the whole loop keeps the tension pattern alive until new, integrable input (e.g., a precise TTC contact) breaks the cycle.

ChatGPT said:
2.2 The Compression Myth and the Rise of Tension‑Based Insight

Modern neuroscience refutes the idea that nerves are typically compressed by misaligned bones. Dr. Heidi Haavik has demonstrated that neural interference more often stems from tension, distortion, and altered afferent input rather than outright compression (Haavik & Murphy 2007). Decades earlier, Dr. Alf Breig showed that meningeal and spinal‑cord tension can disturb neural function even when no vertebral displacement is present, shifting chiropractic thinking from a compression paradigm to one of tension‑based interference (Breig 1978).

Key research milestones:

• Alf Breig (1978) – Documented “Adverse Mechanical Tension” in the meninges; proved that mechanical stretch alone is sufficient to alter neural physiology.
• Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy (2007) – Demonstrated that changes in cortical processing follow distorted afferent input, not nerve pinch; interference is primarily tonal and afferent in origin.

Allostatic load, not pathology
When incoming stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU tightens as an allostatic response, reducing range of motion so less sensory data floods the system (see section 3.2 b). This protective tension is not pathological at first, but it distorts neural communication in two ways:

Misinformation – Hyper‑toned tissues generate skewed sensory data.

Missing information – Restricted motion silences normal proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways.

Together, these changes disrupt afferent and efferent signaling—proprioceptive, interoceptive, autonomic, and motor—between brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. If it persists, the protective pattern becomes maladaptive, laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathology.

Bottom line: interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting the focus of chiropractic from bones to tone.

Interference is primarily afferent distortion, rooted in tone.

Allostatic load, not pathology
When incoming stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU tightens as an allostatic response, reducing range of motion so less sensory data floods the system (see section 3.2 b). This protective tension is not pathological at first, but it distorts neural communication in two ways:
	1.	Misinformation – Hyper‑toned tissues generate skewed sensory data.
	2.	Missing information – Restricted motion silences normal proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways.

Together, these changes disrupt afferent and efferent signaling—proprioceptive, interoceptive, autonomic, and motor—between brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. If it persists, the protective pattern becomes maladaptive, laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathology.

Bottom line: interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting the focus of chiropractic from bones to tone.

Section 2 · From Bone‑on‑Nerve to Tone‑First — A Historical Evolution
2.0 Opening Perspective
“What if the real mechanism of subluxation has been hiding in plain sight?”

From 1895 onward, chiropractic focused on the vertebral subluxation as a bone‑on‑nerve problem. That framework produced life‑changing clinical results, yet it masked a deeper truth first hinted at by D. D. Palmer:

“Life is an expression of tone; the cause of disease is any variation in tone.”
— The Chiropractor’s Adjuster (1910)

Decade by decade, evidence has moved the profession from compression to tension and now to a tone‑first paradigm—setting the stage for the NeuroSpinal model at the center of Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC).

2.1 The Traditional Subluxation Model—Compression First
Historically, vertebral subluxation was defined as

A misalignment or fixation of one or more spinal vertebrae

Interfering with nerve transmission via mechanical compression or irritation

Causing downstream dysfunction and dis‑ease in the body

This bone‑centric view inspired motion palpation, segmental X‑ray listings, and high‑velocity adjustments. Yet it never explained why a joint would fixate under stress or how shifts in neurological tone shape health.

2.2 The Compression Myth and the Rise of Tension‑Based Insight
Modern neuroscience refutes the notion that nerves are routinely pinched by misaligned bones. Dr. Heidi Haavik showed that neural interference more often stems from tension, distortion, and altered afferent input than from outright compression (Haavik & Murphy 2007). Decades earlier, Dr. Alf Breig demonstrated that meningeal and cord tension can disturb neural function even when vertebrae remain properly aligned, ushering chiropractic from a compression paradigm to a tension‑based model (Breig 1978).

• Alf Breig (1978) – documented “Adverse Mechanical Tension”: excess stretch or slack in meninges and cord distorts neural firing without vertebral displacement; mechanical tension alone can cause interference.
• Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy (2007) – somatosensory‑evoked‑potential research showed cortical processing changes follow altered afferent input and meningeal tension—not nerve pinch; interference is primarily tonal and afferent in origin.

Allostatic load, not pathology. When incoming stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU tightens as an allostatic response (i.e., an energy‑using adjustment to ongoing stress), reducing range of motion so less sensory data floods the system (see Section 3.2 b). This protective tension is not pathological at first, but it distorts neural communication in two ways:

1. Misinformation—hyper‑toned tissues generate skewed sensory data.
2. Missing information—restricted motion silences normal proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways.

Combined, these distort afferent and efferent signaling—proprioceptive, interoceptive, autonomic, and motor—between brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. If it persists, the protective pattern becomes maladaptive, laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathology (structural or functional change that qualifies as disease rather than reversible adaptation).

Bottom line: interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting chiropractic from bones to tone.

2.3 Foundational Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic
Era / Technique	Tonal Contribution
Logan Basic (1931)	Low‑force sacral contacts to balance postural tone.
Directional Non‑Force (1940s)	Pressure testing & leg checks as reflex indicators.
Upper Cervical / Grostic (1930s‑80s)	Dentate‑ligament tension hypothesis—segmental misalignment ↔ global dural tone.
Toftness (1950s)	Electromagnetic field detection of interference.
Thompson (1955)	Reflex leg checks later reframed as tone signals.
Network Spinal Analysis (1987)	Wave dynamics that reflect neural re‑organization.
(Expanded historical notes appear in Appendix A.)

2.4 OsseoTonal Techniques—Blending Structure and Tone
Starting in the mid‑1990s, a new class of methods fused articular contacts with tonal analysis. These OsseoTonal techniques confirmed that precise intent and neurological indicators enhance outcomes even when correction is delivered through bone.

Technique	Distinguishing Features
Torque Release Technique (1995)	Instrument‑assisted contacts; pressure testing plus leg checks; tonal intent.
MC2 (1996)	Tonal balance via upper‑cervical contacts and reflex indicators.
Bio‑Geometric Integration (1995)	Biotensegrity & wave dynamics guide osseous contacts.
Mastery Love Service; Kairos; Syntropy; TIC; Pneuma	Blend precise osseous vectors with tonal presence, breath, or flow‑state principles.
2.5 Emergence of the NeuroSpinal Model—Talsky Tonal Chiropractic
Recognizing that bone contacts still approached tone indirectly, Dr. Marvin Talsky introduced Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) in 2001:

Focus: direct engagement with the Cranio‑Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C‑SMFU)

Analysis: tonal pressure testing and leg checks identify the Best Window In

Delivery: least force, most precise direction of ease parallel to aberrant tension

Verification: immediate reflex change proves the body—not the practitioner—initiates correction

This clarified that subluxation begins as a global tone distortion; vertebral misalignment is only a downstream compensation.

2.6 Key Take‑Aways
Bone‑on‑nerve compression is insufficient to explain modern findings.

Aberrant NeuroSpinal tone is the primary interference; vertebral misalignment is secondary.

The C‑SMFU supplies the missing anatomical‑physiological mechanism linking stress adaptation to structural change.

TTC is the first fully non‑articular protocol to engage that mechanism directly.

Next: Section 3 details the anatomy and physiology of the C‑SMFU—the fountainhead of tone.

Here’s the quickest way to adjust the font for any cells that use the “Body” text style in Apple Numbers:

Select the cells
• Click one cell that already contains body text, then choose Edit ▶ Select All Matching Text if you want every cell with that style, or just drag to highlight the range you need.

Open the Format sidebar
• Make sure the right‑hand sidebar is visible (choose View ▶ Show Format if it’s hidden).
• Click the Text tab (second icon, looks like “T”).

Change the font
• At the top of the Text pane you’ll see the current font name and size (e.g., Georgia 11).
• Click the font dropdown and pick Times New Roman (or any font you prefer).
• Adjust the size just to the right (type 12 and press Return for 12 pt).

Update the Body style (optional)
If you want every “Body” cell in this sheet—or future sheets—to adopt the new font automatically:
• In the sidebar, click the paragraph‑style dropdown just above the font name (it likely reads “Body”).
• Choose the little right‑arrow ▸ next to “Body” and select Redefine Style from Selection.
• Now every cell using that Body style updates to your new font and size.

Check line spacing (if needed)
• Still in the Text pane, open the Line Spacing pop‑up (it may read “Single,” “1.15,” etc.).
• Choose “Exactly” and type 1.2 or “Multiple” 1.15 for the spacing you want.

That’s it—your Numbers sheet now uses the professional font and size you need.

define aberrant

Aberrant means deviating from what is normal, expected, or typical.

In clinical and scientific contexts, it refers to a pattern or behavior that is irregular or abnormal, but not necessarily pathological on its own.

In your context (chiropractic / tonal neurology):
Aberrant tone = tone that is dysregulated, excessive, insufficient, or mispatterned compared to the body’s normal adaptive tone.

Quick definitions:
Term	Definition	Example
Aberrant	Deviating from the expected or normal	“Aberrant tension in the meninges disrupts normal sensory flow.”
Normal	Within adaptive, self-regulating ranges	“Normal tone adjusts with posture, breath, and emotion.”
So when you say “aberrant tone”, you're referring to tone that no longer serves functional adaptation and begins to interfere with normal signaling, posture, or perception—even if no disease is present yet.

1. Executive Summary
“Subluxation is always global, and the primary is always NeuroSpinal.”

The science of chiropractic is evolving—and so must our understanding of subluxation. For over a century, the profession largely focused on vertebral subluxation: bones misaligned or fixated, compressing nerves and disrupting function. While that bone-first lens provided a structural foundation, modern anatomy, neurophysiology, and decades of clinical practice now reveal a far more global phenomenon.

This white paper substantiates a paradigm shift: subluxation is fundamentally a state of aberrant tone in the NeuroSpinal System—also known as the Cranio-Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C‑SMFU). This continuous, integrative system comprises the brain, spinal cord, multi-layered meninges, cerebrospinal fluid, and the dural attachments that tether these tissues throughout the cranium and spine. When physical, chemical, or emotional stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU contracts. That contraction generates aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, throttling sensory bandwidth, limiting adaptability, and fragmenting the body’s coherence—often preceding any visible bone misalignment (Breig, 1978; Ward, 1980).

Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) builds on foundational chiropractic theory, decades of hands-on experience, and modern neuroscience. TTC recognizes vertebral subluxation not as the primary interference but as a compensatory adaptation to global neurological tension. Correction is achieved not through high-force mobilization of fixations, but through information-rich input delivered at a precise, non-articular contact aligned with the direction of ease—the vector parallel to the aberrant tension within the C‑SMFU.

Supported by research from Breig (1978), Ward (1980), Haavik (2007), Oschman (2000), Becker & Seldon (1985), and others, TTC reframes the chiropractic adjustment as a neurological communication—a touch-based dialogue, delivered with congruent intent, that facilitates the body to reinitiate the process of self-adjusting, correcting, and returning to wholeness.

This paper will:

Trace the historical evolution of tonal chiropractic thought

Present the anatomical and physiological basis of the C‑SMFU

Examine the distinction between OsseoTonal and direct tonal engagement

Explore information-driven pathways through which the body organizes, adapts, and corrects

Clarify how TTC integrates with and enhances existing chiropractic techniques

In doing so, we offer a lens for the future of principled chiropractic—precise, neurologically grounded, and built on the body’s innate capacity for self-adjustment, healing, and optimization.

To add a vertical line indent (also called a quote bar or block quote line) in Mac Pages, follow these steps:

✅ Option 1: Use the "Quote" style (fastest, built-in method)
Select the text you want to format (e.g., the quote and attribution).

In the Format sidebar, go to the Style tab.

Click the paragraph style dropdown (usually says "Body," "Heading," etc.).

Choose “Quote” from the list.

📎 This will:

Add a subtle vertical gray line to the left,

Indent the paragraph,

Slightly change the font size and style (you can customize that if needed).

✏️ Optional: Customize the Quote style
To change the font/size/spacing of the quote bar:

Select the quote after applying the “Quote” style.

Adjust font, size, or italics in the Format panel as desired.

Click the style dropdown (e.g., “Quote” ▸) and choose “Redefine Style from Selection” to apply your formatting to all quotes.

🛠 Option 2: Manual workaround (if you want custom control)
If you want more custom indentation with a vertical line (like in Word or LaTeX), you can:

Insert a 1-column, 2-row table.

Put the vertical line (a pipe symbol |) in the first cell.

Put your quote in the second cell.

Remove borders, resize column widths, and adjust padding to your liking.

But for 99% of white paper or academic use, the Quote style in Pages is clean, professional, and easily exportable to PDF or Word.

2.1 The Traditional Subluxation Model: Compression First
For most of chiropractic’s history, the subluxation was defined in structural terms:

A misalignment or fixation of one or more vertebrae

Interfering with nerve function via direct mechanical compression or irritation

Causing downstream dysfunction and dis-ease in the body

This structural model—sometimes summarized as “bone out of place, pressing on a nerve”—shaped analysis techniques such as motion palpation, radiographic listings, and high-velocity adjustments. It also framed the adjustment as a physical correction of structural interference.

While this model helped establish chiropractic’s early identity and produced remarkable clinical outcomes, it offered no explanation for why certain vertebrae fixate, how that fixation is maintained neurologically, or what role stress, tone, and adaptability play in the subluxation process.

These gaps set the stage for the tension-interference discoveries that reshaped chiropractic thinking in the late 20th century—leading from compression to tension, and ultimately to tone-first paradigms.

✅ This version:

Retains your clear historical language.

Sharpens the distinctions between the traditional model and what’s to come.

Ends with a natural bridge to Section 2.2, which introduces Breig and Haavik.

1. Executive Summary

“Subluxation is always global, and the primary is always NeuroSpinal.”

The science of chiropractic is evolving—and so must our understanding of subluxation. For more than a century, the profession focused on the vertebral subluxation: bones misaligned or fixated, compressing nerves and disrupting function. While that bone-first lens provided a structural foundation, modern anatomy, neurophysiology, and decades of clinical practice now reveal a far more global phenomenon.

This white paper substantiates a paradigm shift: subluxation is fundamentally a state of aberrant tone in the NeuroSpinal System—also known as the Cranio-Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C-SMFU). This continuous, integrative system comprises the brain, spinal cord, multi-layered meninges, cerebrospinal fluid, and the dural attachments that tether these tissues throughout the cranium and spine. When physical, chemical, or emotional stress exceeds adaptive capacity, the C‑SMFU contracts. That contraction generates aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, throttling sensory bandwidth, limiting adaptability, and fragmenting the body’s coherence—often preceding any visible bone misalignment (Breig, 1978; Ward, 1980).

Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) builds on foundational chiropractic theory, decades of hands-on experience, and modern neuroscience. TTC recognizes vertebral subluxation not as the primary interference, but as a compensatory adaptation to global neurological tension. Correction is achieved not through high-force mobilization of fixations, but through information-rich input delivered at a precise, non-articular contact aligned with the direction of ease—the vector parallel to the aberrant tension within the C‑SMFU.

Supported by research from Breig (1978), Ward (1980), Haavik (2007), Oschman (2000), Becker and Seldon (1985), and others, TTC reframes the chiropractic adjustment as a neurological communication—a touch-based dialogue, delivered with congruent intent, that facilitates the body to reinitiate the process of self-adjusting, correcting, and returning to wholeness.

2.2 The Compression Myth and the Rise of Tension-Based Insight
Modern neuroscience increasingly challenges the long-held assumption that vertebral misalignment routinely causes neural interference through direct mechanical compression. Dr. Heidi Haavik has demonstrated that interference more often stems from tension, distortion, and altered afferent input—not direct compression of nerve roots (Haavik & Murphy, 2007). Decades earlier, neurosurgeon Dr. Alf Breig showed that tension in the meningeal system and spinal cord can disturb neural function even in the absence of vertebral displacement (Breig, 1978). Together, these findings reframed subluxation from a compression-based model to one rooted in aberrant tension and disrupted signaling.

• Alf Breig (1978) – Documented “Adverse Mechanical Tension”: excess stretch or slack in the meninges and cord can distort neural firing without vertebral displacement; mechanical tension alone can alter nerve function.
• Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy (2007) – Somatosensory-evoked potential research showed that cortical processing changes follow altered afferent input and meningeal tension—not nerve pinch. Interference is primarily tonal and afferent in origin.

Allostatic Load, Not Pathology
When incoming stress exceeds the body’s adaptive capacity, the C-SMFU tightens as an allostatic response (i.e., a protective adaptation that uses energy to maintain function under perceived stress), reducing range of motion so less sensory data floods the system (see Section 3.2b). This protective tension is not pathological at first, but it distorts neural communication in two key ways:

Misinformation — Aberrant tension in the NeuroSpinal System alters afferent and efferent signaling.

Missing information — Reduced motion and contractility silence proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways that normally inform the CNS.

Combined, these distortions impair the accuracy and clarity of communication between the brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. But if it persists, the system can no longer recognize safety, and the protective pattern becomes maladaptive—laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathological changes in structure, function, and adaptability.

Bottom line: Neural interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting the focus of chiropractic from bones to tone.

2.1 The Traditional Subluxation Model: Compression First
For most of chiropractic’s history, the subluxation was defined in structural terms:

A misalignment or fixation of one or more vertebrae

Interfering with nerve function via direct mechanical compression or irritation

Causing downstream dysfunction and dis-ease in the body

This structural model—often summarized as “a bone out of place, pressing on a nerve”—became the dominant clinical framework of 20th-century chiropractic. It guided the development of motion palpation, radiographic listings, and high-velocity adjustments. The adjustment itself was framed as a forceful correction aimed at restoring vertebral position and relieving nerve pressure.

While this model helped establish chiropractic’s early identity and produced meaningful clinical results, it lacked an explanation for why certain vertebrae fixate under stress, how that fixation is neurologically maintained, or what role tone, coherence, and adaptability play in the broader picture of health and interference.

These conceptual gaps set the stage for the discoveries that followed—shifting chiropractic thinking from compression to tension, and ultimately to tone-first paradigms.

🧠 Definition of Meninges
The meninges are the three connective tissue membranes that envelop the brain and spinal cord, providing protection, structural support, and fluid regulation. From innermost to outermost, they are:

Pia mater – A delicate, vascular layer that adheres tightly to the surface of the brain and spinal cord.

Arachnoid mater – A thin, web-like membrane that spans over the pia, with cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) flowing through its subarachnoid space.

Dura mater – The tough, outermost layer that forms dural sleeves and tethers to the skull, vertebrae, and sacrum.

✅ So yes—the meninges include the pia, arachnoid, and dura. When we speak of meningeal tone, in your context we are especially referring to tonal tension in the dura mater and its craniospinal attachments (as emphasized by Breig and Talsky), though technically all three layers are involved in cerebrospinal dynamics and tone regulation.

2.2 The Compression Myth and the Rise of Tension-Based Insight
Modern neuroscience increasingly challenges the long-held assumption that vertebral misalignment routinely causes neural interference through direct mechanical compression. Dr. Heidi Haavik has demonstrated that interference more often stems from tension, distortion, and altered afferent input—not direct compression of nerve roots (Haavik & Murphy, 2007). Decades earlier, neurosurgeon Dr. Alf Breig showed that tension in the meningeal system and spinal cord can disturb neural function even in the absence of vertebral displacement (Breig, 1978). Together, these findings reframed subluxation from a compression-based model to one rooted in aberrant tension and disrupted signaling.

• Alf Breig (1978) – Documented “Adverse Mechanical Tension”: excess stretch or slack in the meninges and cord can distort neural firing without vertebral displacement; mechanical tension alone can alter nerve function.
• Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy (2007) – Somatosensory-evoked potential research showed that cortical processing changes follow altered afferent input and meningeal tension—not nerve pinch. Interference is primarily tonal and afferent in origin.

Allostatic load, not pathology
When incoming stress exceeds the body’s adaptive capacity, the C-SMFU tightens as an allostatic response (i.e., a protective adaptation that uses energy to maintain function under perceived stress), reducing range of motion so less sensory data floods the system (see Section 3.2b). This protective tension is not pathological at first, but it distorts neural communication in two key ways:

Misinformation — Aberrant tension in the NeuroSpinal System alters afferent and efferent signaling.

Missing information — Reduced motion and contractility silence proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways that normally inform the CNS.

Combined, these distortions impair the accuracy and clarity of communication between the brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. But if it persists, the system can no longer recognize safety, and the protective pattern becomes maladaptive—laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathological changes in structure, function, and adaptability.

Bottom line: Neural interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting the focus of chiropractic from bones to tone.

2.2 The Shift from Compression to Tension-Based Interference
Modern neuroscience increasingly challenges the long-held assumption that vertebral misalignment routinely causes neural interference through direct mechanical compression. Dr. Heidi Haavik has demonstrated that interference more often stems from tension, distortion, and altered afferent input—not direct compression of nerve roots (Haavik & Murphy, 2007). Decades earlier, neurosurgeon Dr. Alf Breig showed that tension in the meningeal system and spinal cord can disturb neural function even in the absence of vertebral displacement (Breig, 1978). Together, these findings reframed subluxation from a compression-based model to one rooted in aberrant tension and disrupted signaling.

• Alf Breig (1978) – Documented “Adverse Mechanical Tension”: excess stretch or slack in the meninges and cord can distort neural firing without vertebral displacement; mechanical tension alone can alter nerve function.
• Heidi Haavik & Bernadette Murphy (2007) – Somatosensory-evoked potential research showed that cortical processing changes follow altered afferent input and meningeal tension—not nerve pinch. Interference is primarily tonal and afferent in origin.

2.3 Allostatic Load: A Physiological Model for Aberrant Tone
When incoming stress exceeds the body’s adaptive capacity, the C-SMFU tightens as an allostatic response—a protective adaptation that uses energy to maintain function under perceived stress. This contraction reduces range of motion so that less sensory data floods the system (see Section 3.2b).

At first, this response is not pathological. It is an intelligent attempt by the body to stabilize itself and limit overwhelm. But as tone increases within the NeuroSpinal system, two key distortions emerge:

Misinformation — Aberrant tension in the NeuroSpinal system alters afferent and efferent signaling.

Missing information — Reduced motion and contractility silence proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways that normally inform the CNS.

Together, these distortions impair the accuracy and clarity of communication between the brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. But if it persists, the system can no longer recognize safety, and the protective pattern becomes maladaptive—laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathological changes in structure, function, and adaptability.

Bottom line: Neural interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting the focus of chiropractic from bones to tone.

2.3 Allostatic Load: A Physiological Model for Aberrant Tone
When incoming stress exceeds the body’s adaptive capacity, the C-SMFU tightens as an allostatic response—a protective adaptation that uses energy to maintain function under perceived stress. This contraction reduces range of motion so that less sensory data floods the system (see Section 3.2b).

At first, this response is not pathological. It is an intelligent attempt by the body to stabilize itself and limit overwhelm. But as tone increases within the NeuroSpinal system, two key distortions emerge:

Misinformation — Aberrant tension in the NeuroSpinal system alters afferent and efferent signaling.

Missing information — Increased NeuroSpinal tension restricts movement, narrowing the sensory input from proprioceptive and interoceptive pathways. This reduces the signal diversity and richness required for accurate regulation.

Together, these distortions impair the accuracy and clarity of communication between the brain and body. If the overload resolves, tone normalizes. But if it persists, the system can no longer recognize safety, and the protective pattern becomes maladaptive—laying the groundwork for vertebral compensation and eventual pathological changes in structure, function, and adaptability.

Bottom line: Neural interference stems from aberrant NeuroSpinal tension, not primary compression—redirecting the focus of chiropractic from bones to tone.

2.4 Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic and the Emergence of OsseoTonal Techniques
As tonal awareness deepened within the chiropractic profession throughout the 20th and 21st centuries, multiple techniques contributed foundational insights that expanded the understanding of subluxation beyond the vertebral model. These contributions shaped the path toward a tonal paradigm—where subluxation is seen not merely as mechanical fixation or misalignment, but as a state of altered tone and disrupted neurological coherence.

Simultaneously, a new category of chiropractic technique emerged—blending structural contact with tonal awareness. These techniques engage the nervous system through joint articulations but are directed by tonal indicators and intent. They are collectively referred to as OsseoTonal techniques: an emerging class of approaches that integrate osseous (articular) contact with neurologically driven tonal analysis and correction strategies.

This section outlines two distinct categories:

The foundational contributions to tonal chiropractic, including early tonal awareness and reflexive analysis tools

A growing collection of OsseoTonal techniques, which reflect the profession’s evolving synthesis of structure and tone

ChatGPT said:
Great—let’s continue into the next portion of Section 2.4, detailing the foundational contributions to tonal chiropractic. The following content reflects your most recent draft structure, fully refined language, and precise historical placement:

Foundational Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic
The following techniques represent pivotal contributions to the emergence and refinement of tonal chiropractic within the profession.

Logan Basic Technique (1931): Pioneered by Dr. H.B. Logan, this technique recognized subtle shifts in postural tone and sought to influence global alignment through low-force sacral contacts. Logan Basic is considered the first systematized approach to introduce tonal awareness into chiropractic, emphasizing the relationship between pelvic stability, spinal distortion, and compensatory tension patterns.
(Logan University, https://www.logan.edu)

Directional Non-Force Technique (DNFT) (1940s): Developed by Dr. Richard Van Rumpt, DNFT introduced pressure testing and neurological leg checks as reflexive indicators for analysis and post-adjustment verification. While not created as a tonal technique, DNFT’s use of the body’s feedback to guide correction laid a foundational precedent for later tonal analysis models.
(DNFT Chiropractic, https://www.dnftchiropractic.com)

Upper Cervical Chiropractic (1930s): Introduced by B.J. Palmer and further advanced by Dr. John D. Grostic, this model emphasized the systemic effects of upper cervical misalignment on neurological tone. Grostic’s Dentate Ligament–Cord Distortion Hypothesis (1988) proposed that upper cervical misalignments could induce global dural tension through mechanical stress on the spinal cord. This hypothesis helped lay the conceptual groundwork for tonal chiropractic’s focus on central neural tension.
(Palmer, 1934; Grostic, 1988)

Toftness Technique (1950s): The first entirely tonal chiropractic system, Toftness relied on electromagnetic field detection to locate patterns of interference in the nervous system.
(Toftness Postgraduate School of Chiropractic, https://www.toftness.org)

Thompson Technique (1955): Expanded on Van Rumpt’s leg checks to include multiple reflexive indicators. While primarily structural, several of Thompson’s indicators have since been reframed through the lens of global tone in systems like TTC.
(Thompson Technique Foundation, https://www.thompsontechnique.org; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Pierce-Stillwagon Technique (1963): Developed by Drs. Walter V. Pierce Sr. and Glenn Stillwagon, PST integrated stress-view radiography and early instrumentation with reflex testing and biomechanical analysis. While largely structural in its application, its emphasis on motion as a diagnostic indicator and its influence on later tonal systems (especially TRT) are historically significant.
(PRS Chiropractic, https://prschiropractic.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Network Chiropractic / Network Spinal Analysis (NSA) (1987): Created by Dr. Donald Epstein, NSA synthesized reflexive analysis and wave dynamics to assess and facilitate reorganizational patterns in the spine. The system is distinguished by its use of low-force gateway contacts and the observation of respiratory and somatopsychic wave phenomena. Elements of NSA’s tonal analysis continue to inform the TTC model.
(EpiEnergetics, https://www.epienergetics.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Torque Release Technique (TRT) (1995): Co-founded by Dr. Marvin Talsky and Dr. Jay Holder, TRT introduced a tonal instrument-assisted approach using the Integrator™. It combined pressure testing and leg checks with tonal awareness and application. While originally classified as a tonal system, TRT engages the NeuroSpinal system indirectly through osseous input and is now categorized as an OsseoTonal approach—prompting the later development of TTC as a fully direct tonal model.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Directional Non-Force Technique (DNFT): Developed in the 1940s by Dr. Richard Van Rumpt, DNFT introduced pressure testing and neurological leg checks as reflexive indicators for analysis and post-adjustment verification. While not designed as a tonal technique, its use of the body’s own feedback to guide correction laid a crucial foundation for future tonal analysis models.
(DNFT Chiropractic, https://www.dnftchiropractic.com)

Upper Cervical Chiropractic: Introduced in the 1930s by B.J. Palmer and later refined by Dr. John D. Grostic, Upper Cervical Chiropractic emphasized the systemic neurological impact of misalignment in the upper cervical spine. Grostic’s Dentate Ligament–Cord Distortion Hypothesis (published in 1988) proposed that upper cervical misalignments could induce global dural tension via mechanical stress on the spinal cord. This framework offered one of the earliest biomechanical models linking localized structural disturbance to global tonal shifts.
(Palmer, 1934; Grostic, 1988)

Toftness Technique: Developed in the 1950s, Toftness was the first fully tonal chiropractic system, using electromagnetic field detection to locate interference patterns in the nervous system. It emphasized tonal awareness and energetic sensitivity, departing from joint-based or fixation-oriented models.
(Toftness Postgraduate School of Chiropractic, https://www.toftness.org)

Thompson Technique: Created in 1955 by Dr. Clay Thompson, this technique expanded on Van Rumpt’s leg checks and introduced several reflexive indicators still used today. While largely structural in focus, many of Thompson’s indicators have since been reframed through the lens of global tone in systems such as TTC.
(Thompson Technique Foundation, https://www.thompsontechnique.org; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Pierce-Stillwagon Technique (PST): Developed in 1963 by Drs. Walter V. Pierce Sr. and Glenn Stillwagon, PST integrated “stress view” radiography, reflex testing, and biomechanical motion analysis. While its primary application was structural, its emphasis on dynamic function and early use of instrumentation significantly influenced later tonal systems, including Torque Release Technique.
(PRS Chiropractic, https://prschiropractic.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Network Chiropractic / Network Spinal Analysis (NSA): Created in 1987 by Dr. Donald Epstein, NSA synthesized elements from multiple tonal and reflex-based techniques into a reorganizational model. Its hallmark was the identification of low-force “gateway” contacts and the observation of unique respiratory and somatopsychic wave phenomena. NSA provided one of the most complete tonal analysis systems of its time and continues to influence tonal paradigms today.
(EpiEnergetics, https://www.epienergetics.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Torque Release Technique (TRT): Developed in 1995 by Dr. Marvin Talsky and co-founded with Dr. Jay Holder, TRT introduced a tonal instrument-assisted model using the Integrator™. Though initially framed as a tonal technique, TRT engages the NeuroSpinal system through osseous contact and is now best categorized as an OsseoTonal approach—a distinction that later led to the development of TTC as a fully direct tonal system.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Yes—your intuition is correct, and your phrasing is supported by historical chiropractic theory and evolving neurophysiological understanding.

There have been attempts—particularly in mid-20th century chiropractic—to explain vertebral fixation as a mechanical event with neurological consequences, often attributed to nerve pressure, inflammation, or local tissue stress. However, most of those models stop short of providing a mechanism that links emotional, chemical, or physical stress to why a specific vertebra becomes fixated.

Here’s what the most up-to-date Talsky Tonal Chiropractic white paper offers in response:

“This structural model led to analysis techniques such as motion palpation, radiographic evaluation, and segmental listings... However, it lacked a mechanism for understanding how stress, adaptability, and neurological tone fit into the broader picture.”

“Over time... clinical observation show[s] us that vertebral subluxation is not the cause—it is a compensation. It is the visible, structural response to a deeper, global shift in tone occurring within the [C-SMFU]...”

“When the body becomes overwhelmed by stress... it reacts with a neurological contraction... creating patterns of aberrant tension within the core of the system”
.

So:

Traditional subluxation theory: Attributes joint fixation to mechanical derangement or trauma.

Modern tonal model: Attributes fixation as a secondary compensation for global aberrant tone initiated by stress the system cannot adequately integrate.

To your direct question: No, the classic compression model does not offer a satisfying mechanism that connects stress adaptation to vertebral fixation. That’s why the TTC model is unique and necessary—it redefines subluxation as a protective, tonally driven contraction within the NeuroSpinal system, with vertebral changes being downstream structural adaptations.
