Neurophysiological Mechanisms Behind the Development of Vertebral Subluxation

Neurophysiological Theories of Vertebral Subluxation: Historical and Modern Perspectives
Early Chiropractic Theories (1890s–1920s)
D.D. Palmer (Founder, 1895): The original chiropractic theory by Daniel David Palmer posited that health is governed by the nervous system's “tone” – the degree of nerve tension and vibration. Palmer wrote, “Life is the expression of tone… Tone is the normal degree of nerve tension… the cause of disease is any variation of tone—nerves too tense or too slack.”
pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov
 In Palmer’s view, vertebral subluxation (a slight misalignment or biomechanical derangement of a spinal segment) disturbs this tone by impinging spinal nerves and thus “disconnecting” the flow of vital nerve impulses (what he called “mental impulses” or innate intelligence)
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Palmer identified physical, chemical, and emotional stresses – famously dubbed the “Three T’s” (Trauma, Toxins, Thoughts) – as precipitating causes of subluxations
drannejensen.com
theamericanchiropractor.com
. In other words, accidents or postural injuries (traumas), exposure to poisons or biological toxins (toxins), and even mental or emotional strain (thoughts or autosuggestion) could alter nervous system tone and cause a vertebra to become subluxated. B.J. Palmer (Developer, early 1900s): D.D.’s son, Bartlett Joshua Palmer, expanded and zealously promoted the subluxation theory. B.J. Palmer maintained that a subluxated vertebra causes nerve compression or irritation that interferes with neural transmission, ultimately leading to “abnormal functions” and disease
en.wikipedia.org
. He codified the classic definition: “a vertebral subluxation means pressure on nerves [and] abnormal functions creating a lesion in some portion of the body”
en.wikipedia.org
. B.J. insisted that nerve interference was the critical factor – without it, a misalignment was not a true chiropractic subluxation
chiro.org
. In clinical practice, he focused on the upper cervical spine (the atlas/axis region), hypothesizing that misalignments there could compress the brainstem or upper spinal cord (what he termed the “atlas subluxation complex” or “cord pressure” theory). This upper-cervical concept, known as the “Hole-in-One” (HIO) technique, held that a major C1–C2 subluxation could produce widespread neurophysiological effects by disturbing cerebrospinal fluid flow or stressing the spinal cord meninges (via the dentate ligaments)
pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov
. Early chiropractic writings often described subluxation in almost mechanistic terms – likening nerve impingement to a “pinched nerve” or a “foot on a hose” blocking nerve supply – and taught that adjusting the misaligned vertebra restores proper nerve tone and health
chiro.org
chiro.org
. R.W. Stephenson (1927): In the first chiropractic textbook (1927), Dr. Ralph Stephenson synthesized Palmer’s ideas into 33 principles. He defined the vertebral subluxation with four hallmark criteria: (1) misalignment of a vertebra with the one above or below, (2) occlusion of an opening (e.g. narrowing of the intervertebral foramen), (3) nerve impingement, and (4) interference with the transmission of mental impulses
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Stephenson insisted that nerve interference (disrupted communication between brain and body) is what makes the lesion a true subluxation. His influential definition reinforced that a subluxation is not merely a slight anatomical misalignment, but a functional neurophysiological lesion that disturbs the body’s innate communication networks
chiro.org
. Notably, Stephenson echoed D.D. Palmer’s view on stressors, stating that subluxations can arise from “toxins, trauma, and autosuggestion,” paralleling the Three T’s causes of disease. Early Divergent Views: While the Palmers’ nerve-compression paradigm dominated early chiropractic, there were contemporaries who offered alternative mechanistic explanations. In 1906, Solon Langworthy, Minora Paxson, and Oakley Smith published Modernized Chiropractic, suggesting that subluxation involved not just a “bone out of place,” but also joint fixations and ligamentous or muscular dysfunction. They described the lesion as a “partial dislocation” accompanied by changes in surrounding soft tissues and a reduction of normal motion
chiro.org
. This was a departure from the pure nerve pinching idea – Langworthy’s camp believed a stuck or hypomobile vertebra could cause ill health by disturbing reflexes and circulation. However, D.D. Palmer dismissed these notions at the time (labeling such ideas “mixer” deviations). Still, the seed was planted for later generations to view subluxation in terms of functional joint restriction and not only static misalignment. Early osteopathic medicine (founded 1874 by A.T. Still) had a parallel concept of the “osteopathic lesion,” emphasizing impaired vertebral motion and circulation; this likely influenced or resonated with chiropractic “mixers” of the era.
Mid-20th Century Developments (1930s–1960s)
By the mid-20th century, chiropractic theorists increasingly incorporated biomechanical and soft-tissue perspectives, moving toward an integrated neurophysiological model of subluxation. Key figures in this period include: Joseph Janse (1940s): Dr. Joseph Janse, an influential chiropractor and educator (President of National College of Chiropractic), advanced a modernized definition of subluxation. Janse emphasized abnormal joint motion and fixation as central. His view was that a subluxation exists when a vertebra’s motion is sufficiently aberrant that the segment becomes locked in a misaligned position, which can then produce nerve pressure or irritation
chiro.org
. In his words, “a subluxation occurs when abnormal motion is exceeded and osseous movement causes pressure on a nerve. The essential feature… is that the vertebra is relatively fixed in its abnormal position and no longer takes part in the normal movement of the spine. If a vertebra possesses normal mobility, it is not considered subluxated.”
chiro.org
 Thus, fixation and loss of mobility were key – a concept foreshadowed by earlier mixers. Janse taught that the goal of an adjustment was to restore motion to a “locked” spinal segment: “The cure of disease by adjustment… is accomplished by the conversion of rigid segments into moveable ones.”
chiro.org
 This paradigm shifted focus from bones out of place squeezing nerves, to dysfunctional joints causing neurologic disturbance. Henri Gillet and Fixation Theory: In Europe, Belgian chiropractor Dr. Henri Gillet (mid-20th century) elaborated the fixation/dyskinesia model through meticulous study of spinal motion. Gillet used techniques like cineradiography (motion X-rays) and palpation to show that certain vertebral segments become hypomobile (“fixed”) after trauma or strain, even if static x-rays show minimal misalignment. He proposed that such fixations alter local mechanoreceptor input and cause muscular and vascular changes that can irritate nerves. Gillet’s fixation theory held that a “vertebral subluxation” in chiropractic is essentially a functional locking of the joint with resultant reflex changes – what later would be termed a “vertebral fixation” or joint dysfunction. In fact, as chiropractic researcher Dr. Raymond Sandoz noted, the term “chiropractic subluxation” evolved to become practically synonymous with any functional spinal derangement (static or dynamic, including fixation and instability) by the mid-1900s
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Sandoz argued that to avoid confusion, the original meaning (“partial dislocation”) should be distinguished from purely dynamic lesions; he suggested using the term “vertebral fixation” for the chiropractic concept of a purely functional (reversible) joint derangement
chiro.org
. While terminology was debated, clinicians like Gillet firmly established that restricted segmental mobility and altered joint play were critical signs of subluxation. Gillet’s work, along with Fred Illi and others, also documented how such fixations could produce local inflammation and pain, and how releasing the fixation (adjustment) normalized motion and relieved symptoms – evidence of a somatic reflex phenomenon at the level of the lesion. Reflex and Soft-Tissue Components: The 1940s–60s also saw important interdisciplinary contributions. Osteopathic researcher Dr. J. Stedman Denslow in 1947 recorded measurable increases in paraspinal muscle activity (spasm) at segments with osteopathic lesions (somatic dysfunction), suggesting a segmental reflex facilitation. Similarly, chiropractors began to incorporate knowledge of muscle and connective tissue involvement. Dr. Janse’s 1947 textbook (co-authored with M. W. R. Postles and E. J. Thomas) acknowledged that muscles, ligaments, and inflammation around the joint play roles in sustaining a subluxation. In 1950, chiropractor Dr. Verner Homewood described a “neurovascular disturbance” model: trauma to a joint causes soft-tissue injury and vascular congestion, which in turn irritate nerves – a cycle that can persist until corrected. This era also introduced the idea of trophic nerve changes: subluxation might not sever nerves, but could cause subtle neuropathies or block axoplasmic flow (transport of nutrients along the nerve fiber), leading to dysregulated tissue growth or immune function. Soviet physiologist A. D. Speransky’s work on neurotrophic effects (1930s) and Dr. Chung Ha Suh’s later research on altered nerve signaling under compression (1960s) lent credence to these “nerve dysfunction” hypotheses beyond simple pinch. By 1960, the full “subluxation complex” was being appreciated – a combination of spinal misalignment or fixation, nervous system involvement (compression or irritation), muscle spasm/imbalance, inflammation, and even distant effects on organ systems. This set the stage for formal models in the 1970s that integrated all these elements.
The Shift to Neurophysiological Models (1970s)
The mid-1970s marked a turning point where chiropractic theory was re-examined through the lens of contemporary neurophysiology. A landmark event was the National Institutes of Health (NIH) Conference on Spinal Manipulative Therapy in 1975, which gathered chiropractors, osteopaths, and medical scientists. It highlighted the paucity of rigorous research into subluxation and spurred new scientific inquiry
chiro.org
. Around this time, several models emerged that attempted to explain how subluxations form and persist via neurological mechanisms:
Korr’s Segmental Facilitation (1970s): Perhaps the most influential interdisciplinary theory came from osteopathic researcher Dr. Irvin Korr. Korr proposed that a chronically “stuck” spinal segment (what DOs called a somatic dysfunction or lesion, equivalent to a subluxation) leads to a state of segmental facilitation in the spinal cord. In facilitated segments, interneurons in the cord become hyper-responsive due to sustained bombardment of nociceptive and mechanoreceptive impulses from the dysfunctional segment
chiro.org
. The result is a lowered threshold for firing in both somatic efferents (motor neurons) and sympathetic efferents at that level
chiro.org
. Korr noted that such lesions are associated with “exaggerated sympathetic activity as well as exaggerated paraspinal muscle tone.”
chiro.org
 In other words, a subluxation could cause a vicious cycle of increased gamma motor drive to paraspinal muscles (maintaining spasm and fixation) and heightened sympathetic nerve output (affecting visceral function). It’s notable that Korr, like D.D. Palmer decades before, emphasized “tone” – he described the facilitated segment as having abnormally high nervous system tone (sympathetic overdrive and muscle tension)
chiro.org
chiro.org
. According to Korr, chronic sympathetic over-excitation from spinal lesions could alter organ function and lower resistance to disease: “High sympathetic tone may alter organ and tissue responses to hormones, infectious agents, and blood components.”
chiro.org
 This hinted at an immunological dimension (which later research explored). Korr’s model explained how physical stress or injury could initiate a self-sustaining reflex loop: an initial trauma strains a segment’s soft tissues → triggers pain receptors and muscle spindles → the spinal cord amplifies and perpetuates the signals (facilitation) → paraspinal muscles splint the area (lock the joint) and sympathetic nerves fire excessively → local tissues become trophically and chemically altered, further exciting sensory nerves – a persistent “fixation reflex.” This gamma loop feedback was a plausible neurophysiological process for subluxation development and maintenance
chiro.org
chiro.org
.
Denslow’s and Korr’s Research: Korr’s ideas were supported by prior laboratory findings. Denslow had demonstrated that simulated lesions in animals led to persistent reflex activity in segmental muscles. In a series of studies, Korr and colleagues showed that when certain intersegmental muscles were experimentally shortened or irritated, the spinal cord segment developed a facilitated state where even slight additional stimuli elicited exaggerated responses (the “central sensitization” phenomenon). They also found that such segmental dysfunctions could be associated with visceral organ changes via sympathetics – providing a biological basis for the chiropractic claim of somatovisceral effects. Japanese researchers A. Sato and Y. Sato later (1980s) corroborated this by showing that noxious stimulation of spinal structures (e.g. facet joint capsule or paraspinal muscles) causes reflex increases in sympathetic output to the heart, blood vessels, and other organs
sciencedirect.com
. These somato-autonomic reflex studies support the idea that a spinal lesion can trigger remote organ dysfunction (e.g. changes in heart rate, blood pressure, gastrointestinal activity), linking back to early chiropractic observations of organic symptoms improving after adjustments.
Nerve Compression Revisited: Although chiropractors were moving beyond the simplistic “bone on nerve” model, research in the 1970s shed new light on nerve root compression. Notably, a 1975 NIH-funded study by Sharpless revealed that extremely low pressures can indeed alter nerve root function. Sharpless found that pressure as low as 10 mm Hg on a spinal nerve root reduced its compound action potential by 60% within 15 minutes
chiro.org
. Higher pressures caused greater and more prolonged conduction blocks
chiro.org
. These results were termed “astonishing” because earlier animal studies had suggested very high pressure (hundreds of mm Hg) was needed for nerve conduction block, but those studies used peripheral nerves which are more resilient
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Spinal nerve roots lack a protective perineurium and have poorer microcirculation, making them highly vulnerable to even mild compression
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Dr. Korr highlighted factors like the lack of epineurial protection, single blood-supply, and confined space in the intervertebral foramen, which explain why a small misalignment or osteophyte could produce significant neural dysfunction via venous congestion and edema around the nerve root
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Additionally, researchers like Rydevik showed that 5–10 mm Hg pressure can block venous outflow from nerve roots, causing intraneural edema and “chemical radiculitis” (inflammatory irritation of nerves)
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Thus, physical compression and associated inflammatory responses were recognized as co-factors in subluxation neurophysiology. A mildly subluxated segment (or incipient disc lesion) might not visibly pinch a nerve on X-ray, yet could induce venous stasis and inflammation in the foramen, leading to neural dysfunction and pain. This helped vindicate the classic chiropractic premise (pressure on nerves can affect function), while placing it on a more refined physiological footing (circulatory and inflammatory factors). As one review concluded, the once-maligned “garden hose” hypothesis was “far from obsolete,” even if an oversimplification
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Nerve root compression is now seen as one of many possible mechanisms by which a subluxation may produce neurological effects
chiro.org
.
Emergence of the Vertebral Subluxation Complex: In the late 1970s, chiropractors like Dr. Leonard Faye began teaching a multi-dimensional view of subluxation known as the Vertebral Subluxation Complex (VSC). This concept, later formalized by researchers Dr. John Dishman and Dr. Scott Haldeman (and elaborated by Dr. Joseph Keating and Dr. Christopher Kent), describes the subluxation not as a single pathological entity but as a complex of interrelated components:
Kinesiopathology – abnormal motion or alignment of the spinal segment (hypomobility, hypermobility, or aberrant movement patterns)
chiro.org
.
Neuropathology – nerve system changes, which could include nerve root compression, altered sensory input, or reflexive dysregulation of the CNS
chiro.org
.
Myopathology – muscle spasm, weakness, or imbalance associated with the segment
chiro.org
.
Histopathology – tissue changes such as inflammation, edema, and degeneration in and around the joint
chiro.org
.
Biochemical changes – local biochemical alterations (e.g. release of inflammatory cytokines, changes in nerve neurotransmitters) that accompany chronic dysfunction
chiro.org
.
This five-component VSC model (attributed to Faye’s 1982 notes and the LACC “Green Book” by Flesia) provided a broader framework to teach chiropractors that a subluxation is more than a bone out of place – it’s a pathophysiological complex involving many systems
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Soon after, Dr. John Lantz expanded the model to nine components, explicitly adding elements like angiology (microcirculatory changes), inflammatory response, and pathophysiology of organs as part of the subluxation complex
chiro.org
. Lantz stated his aim was a “unified field theory” of chiropractic that integrates every aspect of clinical findings into one model
chiro.org
. Indeed, common to all these models was the understanding (as Lantz wrote) that “every concept of subluxation” involves some form of kinesiologic dysfunction and some form of neurologic involvement
chiro.org
. By 1990, the chiropractic colleges (via the Association of Chiropractic Colleges) adopted a position paper defining vertebral subluxation in similar terms – as a complex involving compromised biomechanical function and neural integrity, having potentially broad effects on health.
Thematic Mechanisms of Subluxation Formation
Across the historical and contemporary theories, several key neurophysiological mechanisms have been proposed to explain how subluxations form in response to physical, chemical, or emotional stress. Below we summarize these thematic mechanisms and their proponents:
Mechanical Injury and Inflammation: Physical trauma (such as a fall, lifting injury, or repetitive strain) can damage the stabilizing tissues of a spinal joint – including muscles, ligaments, discs, and facet capsules. The body’s response to such micro-injury is inflammation: release of prostaglandins, substance P, and other inflammatory mediators that cause pain and swelling. In the acute phase, this inflammation triggers protective muscle splinting (spasm) around the joint. What begins as an adaptive guarding response can become maladaptive if the segment remains restricted. The sustained inflammation and edema can sensititize local nociceptors (pain fibers) causing persistent pain signals into the spinal cord. This scenario aligns with the “injury -> inflammation -> reflex spasm -> joint fixation” pathway. Dr. Scott Haldeman described how a spinal subluxation might present with pain, muscle tension and reduced mobility, which in turn cause reflexively increased muscle tone and even sympathetic hyperactivity
chiro.org
. He noted that the increased afferent input from an injured/fixated segment could trigger central nervous system effects – for example, activation of the reticular formation leading to insomnia or heightened stress response
chiro.org
. In other words, pain and biomechanical disorder at one segment might contribute to global symptoms like sleep disturbance or anxiety via central pathways – a plausible mechanism for stress-related subluxation outcomes. Chronic inflammation around the joint can also lead to fibrosis and degenerative changes (per the “Subluxation Degeneration” model, which observes that long-standing fixations often show early osteoarthritis changes). This further restricts motion and can entrap nerve roots or irritate dorsal root ganglia chemically. Contemporary research indeed ties spine degeneration to mechanoreceptor and nociceptor irritation and segmental instability
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Dr. Daniel Seaman in the 1990s emphasized the role of inflammation in joint dysfunction, noting that pro-inflammatory diets or systemic inflammation (“chemical stress”) can sensitize the nervous system and predispose spinal segments to pain and dysfunction. His work encouraged chiropractors to consider nutritional and lifestyle factors (e.g. high sugar diet leading to chronic inflammation) as contributing to the subluxation complex via neurochemical pathways.
Mechanoreceptor & Proprioceptive Insult (Dysafferentation): A very influential modern hypothesis is that vertebral subluxation represents a state of sensory dysregulation. The articular, muscular, and ligamentous structures of the spine are richly endowed with mechanoreceptors and proprioceptive nerve endings
chiro.org
chiro.org
. When a joint moves properly, these receptors send a healthy pattern of impulses to the spinal cord and brain, which helps regulate muscle tone, balance, and even pain modulation. If a joint becomes hypomobile or misaligned (as in a subluxation/fixation), this normal afferent signaling is disturbed – some receptors (like slow adapting stretch receptors) may be under-stimulated, while others (like nociceptors or “danger” receptors) may fire excessively. The result is a distorted barrage of sensory information to the central nervous system, termed dysafferentation (meaning “bad afferent input”). Chiropractor-scientists Dr. Christopher Kent and Dr. D. R. Seaman both wrote seminal papers in the 1990s on this “dysafferentation model”
chiro.org
chiro.org
. They explained that joint complex dysfunction alters normal proprioception and nociception, leading to what Kent analogized as a “garbage in – garbage out” scenario for the brain
chiro.org
chiro.org
. The aberrant sensory input can cause the central nervous system to generate dysponesis – maladaptive motor or autonomic outputs (garbage out) that further perpetuate the problem. For example, if a cervical facet joint is fixated, the lack of normal mechanoreceptor firing from that joint, combined with extra nociceptive input, can cause the brain to misjudge the body’s position and tension. This may manifest as tight neck muscles, altered posture, or even dizziness or headache (through cervicogenic reflexes). Dr. Michael Freeman and others have noted that such proprioceptive disturbances can corrupt motor programs and “spatial awareness” of the body, contributing to injury and chronic pain cycles
chiro.org
. Anatomical studies by Bogduk and Windsor showed cervical discs and facets have dual innervation (somatic and sympathetic) and abundant free nerve endings
chiro.org
chiro.org
, indicating that a spinal dysfunction can simultaneously trigger pain pathways and sympathetic (stress) responses. Wyke and McLain’s research on facet joint receptors demonstrated that these mechanoreceptors play a crucial role in protecting joints from instability and modulating pain
chiro.org
chiro.org
. When their function is disturbed, joint degeneration and pain perception can worsen. Additionally, the concept of a “neural image” (from the 1960s work Segmental Neuropathy at CMCC) posited that the brain holds an internal mapping of the body based on afferent input, and if subluxation distorts that input, the brain’s control over that body part degrades
chiro.org
. Modern neuroplasticity research, spearheaded by scientists like Dr. Heidi Haavik, has provided evidence for this model: In individuals with subclinical neck pain (mild undiagnosed dysfunction), Haavik found altered somatosensory evoked potentials and poorer joint position sense, implying their brain was not accurately sensing or controlling the affected area
ncmicfoundation.org
ncmicfoundation.org
. After chiropractic adjustments, improvements in sensorimotor integration and reflex responses have been observed, supporting the idea that correcting dysafferentation is a key mechanism of chiropractic care. In summary, the mechanoreceptor/dysafferentation model explains subluxation as a neuroplastic phenomenon – an initial bout of joint dysfunction causes abnormal sensory feedback, which leads to adaptive changes in the central nervous system that can entrench the dysfunction as a learned pattern (sometimes called “spinal learning” or memory)
researchgate.net
researchgate.net
. It highlights why even after the original injury heals, the segment may remain functionally subluxated until the aberrant feedback loop is broken (often by manual adjustment, which floods the CNS with normalizing mechanoreceptive input).
Muscle Spindle “Gamma Loop” and Fixation Reflex: Closely related to dysafferentation is the specific role of muscle spindles and the gamma motor system in mediating subluxation. Korr’s work in the 1970s emphasized that a small spinal injury or strain can cause overactivation of gamma motor neurons, which increases the sensitivity (“gain”) of muscle spindles in that segment. Consequently, the affected muscles become hypertonic and resist stretching, effectively locking the joint in place. This mechanism is often referred to as the gamma loop hypothesis. It provides a neat explanation for those cases where a seemingly minor incident leads to a chronic fixated segment: the trauma triggers an exaggerated spindle reflex that never fully shuts off, continually telling the intrinsics muscles to contract and “splint” the joint. Over time, this aberrant reflex can even spread to multiple segments (a facilitated segment can recruit neighboring levels) and can cause referred pain or autonomic changes. Animal experiments by Patterson and Steinmetz in the 1980s demonstrated that spinal reflexes can become “learned” or sustained long after the initial stimulus, especially if the dorsal horn is bombarded with repeated signals – essentially a form of use-dependent plasticity. This offers a neurological explanation for the long-observed phenomenon that chronic subluxations tend to recur easily: the spinal cord has been trained to maintain that circuit, akin to a memory. The fixated joint, by depriving the cord of normal movement signals, fails to extinguish the reflex. Chiropractic adjustments help by mechanically stretching the joint and muscles, which can reset the muscle spindle gain (via sudden stretch, stretching intrafusal fibers) and send strong afferent input up the cord that can disrupt the reflex arc. This is why patients often feel muscles “release” or mobility improve immediately after an adjustment – the intervention has interrupted the pathological loop. In summary, the fixation-reflex model (muscle spindles and interneurons) accounts for how physical stresses (like a heavy lift or awkward twist) lead to a locked joint via reflexive muscle guarding, and how that lock-in persists neurologically. It’s supported by the work of Korr, Denslow, and modern pain science showing central sensitization at the spinal cord level.
“Tone” Dysregulation and the Tonal Model: The concept of tone that D.D. Palmer introduced has seen a renaissance in some modern chiropractic approaches, often termed tonal models. Beyond the mechanical joint, these models focus on the overall tone or tension in the nervous system and its distribution. Techniques like Network Spinal Analysis (developed by Donny Epstein) and Torque Release Technique (Jay Holder) view subluxations as indicators of global “neural efficiency” or strain patterns in the body’s emotional and physiological state. They draw from the idea that stress (physical, emotional, chemical) can cause “neural distortion” or dysponesis – a reversible physiological state where the nervous system is producing uncoordinated outputs (such as chronic muscle tension, postural distortion, or dysregulated organ function)
irispublishers.com
. Dr. Christopher Kent describes dysponesis as “errors in energy expenditure due to inappropriate neuromuscular responses to stress”
insightcla.com
. For example, chronic emotional stress might lead to a habitually elevated shoulder or forward head carriage (a muscular reflex to stress) – over time, that posture produces spinal joint stress and fixation (subluxation). The subluxation in turn feeds more nociceptive input to the brain, potentially reinforcing the stress response – a self-perpetuating cycle. Tonal chiropractors aim to “unwind” these stress patterns by gentle input (soft touches or instrument taps) that cue the nervous system to relax and reposition, rather than forcefully manipulating a single joint. The underlying neurophysiology here overlaps with dysafferentation and segmental facilitation – it’s essentially addressing the autonomic component of subluxation (imbalanced sympathetic/parasympathetic tone). Indeed, Kent and others have identified dysautonomia (autonomic nervous system imbalance) as a feature of vertebral subluxation in some patients
irispublishers.com
. Clinical research using heart rate variability (an autonomic tone indicator) shows that chiropractic adjustments or tonal techniques can sometimes shift a patient from sympathetic dominance toward a more balanced autonomic state
irispublishers.com
. This suggests that part of the subluxation’s effect is modulation of global nervous system tone – too much sympathetic drive (“fight or flight”) or erratic fluctuations. Therefore, one can view subluxation not only as a local biomechanical lesion but also as a neurological state reflecting the overall stress load on a person. D.D. Palmer’s century-old assertion that “disease is a result of too much or too little tone”
pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov
 finds resonance in these modern tonal models and in psychoneuroimmunology research showing that chronic stress (thoughts) and environmental chemicals (toxins) indeed alter neural tone and immune function
chiro.org
chiro.org
.
Neurodystrophic and Neuroimmune Effects: Another theme throughout the history of subluxation theory is the effect of subluxation on the body’s immune and endocrine systems – sometimes called the neurodystrophic hypothesis. Early chiropractors observed that patients under care seemed less prone to illnesses, and during the 1918 flu pandemic, there were anecdotal reports of chiropractic-treated patients faring better, spurring the idea that subluxation weakened “tissue resistance” to disease. The neurodystrophic model posits that neural dysfunction leads to lowered tissue immunity and trophic changes
chiro.org
. When a spinal segment is subluxated, the ensuing sympathetic overdrive and altered nerve supply can hamper the immune system’s optimal function and the normal trophic (growth and repair) influence nerves have on tissues
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Korr’s finding that facilitated segments display sympathetic hyperactivity provided one mechanism: chronic sympathetic tone can dysregulate immune organs. In the 1980s, neuroscientists (e.g. Felten) discovered direct sympathetic nerve fibers in lymph nodes and spleen, showing that the nervous system can literally modulate immune cell activity
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Stressful stimuli or pain arising from subluxations could therefore trigger the release of catecholamines and substance P, which in turn alter lymphocyte circulation and cytokine production
chiro.org
chiro.org
. For instance, experiments have shown that acute stress can cause immune cells (natural killer cells, T-cells) to redistribute in the body and change their reactivity
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Chronic sympathetic stress is linked to higher susceptibility to infections and slower healing
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Modern chiropractic research, though limited, has explored these connections: Dr. Patricia Brennan’s study in 1991 demonstrated that a single thoracic adjustment led to a significant boost in polymorphonuclear neutrophil (immune cell) activity shortly after the intervention
chiro.org
. Another small pilot study by Selano (1994) observed improved CD4 (T-helper cell) counts in HIV-positive patients receiving upper cervical adjustments over a 6-month period
chiro.org
. While these are preliminary, they support the idea that correcting subluxations might reduce stress on the nervous system and thereby enhance immune function. In sum, the neurodystrophic model provides a theoretical bridge for chemical stressors in subluxation etiology: for example, poor nutrition, allergens, or toxins can create chronic inflammation and oxidative stress that sensitize the nervous system. This leads to neuro-endocrine changes (e.g. elevated cortisol, sympathetic tone) that make the spinal tissues more reactive and prone to facilitation – hence subluxations could more easily develop or persist. It also suggests why emotional stress (via cortisol and adrenaline) might predispose one to subluxations: the immune system and musculoskeletal system are indirectly linked through neural pathways, so high stress can manifest as both tense muscles and suppressed immunity, a combination ripe for joint dysfunction and illness
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Chiropractic writings often cite Selye’s General Adaptation Syndrome – chronic stress exhausts adaptive capacity and “disease of adaptation” follows – with subluxations being one physical representation of the body failing to adapt to stress load. Modern neuroimmunology confirms that nerves, hormones, and immune cells form an integrated response network to stress
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Thus, the presence of a vertebral subluxation might both result from stress and contribute to further stress by keeping the body in a state of heightened alarm (via pain and sympathetic irritation). Correcting the subluxation might break that cycle, allowing a state of ease (homeostasis) to return, which could explain reports of improved well-being and resistance to disease after chiropractic care.
Dural Tension and Cord Distortion: A unique mechanical-neurological mechanism pertains especially to the upper cervical spine and craniosacral dynamics. This is the concept of dural torque or adverse mechanical cord tension. Pioneering work by neurosurgeon Dr. Alf Breig in 1978 demonstrated that malposition of spinal structures can put traction on the spinal cord and brainstem, stretching the dura mater and neural elements
pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov
. Breig showed that straightening of the normal cervical lordosis, for example, can elongate the cord by several centimeters, contributing to neurological deficits. Upper cervical chiropractors (such as those in NUCCA and Atlas Orthogonal techniques) incorporated this into the “dentate ligament–cord distortion hypothesis.” They propose that an atlas subluxation creates a subtle twisting pull on the spinal cord via the dentate ligaments (lateral stabilizing bands of pia mater attaching cord to dura). This dural tension could produce symptoms far from the neck – from headache and trigeminal nerve irritation to dysautonomia – by physically deforming the cord or hindering cerebrospinal fluid flow. While difficult to measure, cadaver studies and some MRI evidence suggest that misalignments can indeed deform neural tissues. This ties back to B.J. Palmer’s HIO notion but with a connective tissue rationale: it may not require direct cord compression, but even slight tensile stress on the cord/dura from a rotated atlas could disturb central neural function. Another example is the sacroiliac joint “Category II” idea in Sacro-Occipital Technique (SOT), where pelvic instability is thought to induce meningeal torsion all the way up the spine. Physical stressors like a whiplash injury or even birth trauma could initiate such dural stress. Chiropractors like Dr. John Grostic investigated these ideas with upper cervical drop-table adjustments aimed at reducing measured leg-length inequality and neurological signs, hypothesizing they were relieving adverse cord tension. This mechanism remains theoretical but is an intriguing aspect of subluxation neurophysiology – emphasizing the continuous tissue connections from spine to brain. It also underscores the importance of posture: poor posture (forward head carriage, etc.) can place the cord under chronic mild stretch, potentially contributing to the feeling of “stress” in the nervous system. Correction of posture and curves (through adjustment and exercise) would then alleviate this tension.
Contemporary Synthesis and Models (2000s–Present)
Modern chiropractic researchers and theoreticians have largely converged on explanations of vertebral subluxation that integrate the above mechanisms into a cohesive whole. A contemporary definition, put forward by the multidisciplinary Rubicon Group of chiropractic colleges in 2017, is as follows:
“A chiropractic subluxation is a self-perpetuating, central segmental motor control problem involving a joint (motion segment) that is not moving appropriately, resulting in ongoing maladaptive neural plastic changes that interfere with the central nervous system’s ability to self-regulate, self-organize, adapt, repair, and heal.”
ncmicfoundation.org
This definition encapsulates several key points: (1) subluxation is seen as a problem of motor control – essentially how the brain and spine are communicating – rather than just a bone out of place; (2) it is self-perpetuating, meaning once it starts it can sustain itself via feedback loops (e.g. reflexes, plasticity); (3) it involves maladaptive neural plastic changes, highlighting the role of the central nervous system in chronically maintaining the dysfunction (a nod to dysafferentation and facilitation models); and (4) it has systemic consequences, impairing the CNS’s normal adaptive capacity (echoing neurodystrophic and tone concepts). In other words, the current view is that a subluxation is fundamentally a neuro-biomechanical dysfunction – part physical joint lesion and part neurological phenomenon – induced by stress and capable of affecting the whole person’s health. Heidi Haavik’s Brain Model: Dr. Heidi Haavik of New Zealand has been a leading figure in researching how spinal joint dysfunction (subluxation) affects the brain. In a series of studies (2007–2018), her team has shown that patients with subluxations or subclinical pain have altered sensorimotor integration in the cortex and cerebellum. For instance, one study found that adjusting dysfunctional cervical segments in asymptomatic young adults led to improved cortical processing of elbow movement (measured via somatosensory evoked potentials and reaction time tasks)
ncmicfoundation.org
ncmicfoundation.org
. Haavik proposes that a subluxation can be viewed as a “central segmental motor control problem” – basically a glitch in the neural circuits that control a spinal segment, caused by incorrect feedback from that segment
ncmicfoundation.org
. Over time this leads to the brain literally anticipating the wrong movements or failing to properly activate stabilizing muscles, thereby reinforcing the joint’s dysfunction. Her work also ties in with pain science: long-standing subluxations may induce mild central sensitization and inefficient motor learning, explaining why people with chronic neck dysfunction show changes in cerebellar-motor cortex communication and are slower at learning new motor tasks
ncmicfoundation.org
. The good news is spinal adjustments seem to reverse some of these changes, at least temporarily – what Haavik calls “rebooting the system” with a surge of afferent input. This modern research uses tools like EEG, EMG, and fMRI to validate that indeed, chiropractic adjustments can produce neuroplastic effects in the brain and spinal cord consistent with normalizing dysafferentation. Such evidence bolsters the credibility of subluxation as a real neurophysiological entity (as opposed to the caricature of a “bone out of place”). It also guides the profession toward an emphasis on quantifiable outcomes (improved proprioception, better balance, improved reflexes) rather than only metaphysical concepts. Christopher Kent’s Models: Dr. Chris Kent has contributed significantly to contemporary theory, coining the “3D model” of subluxation involving Dyskinesia, Dysafferentation, and Dysautonomia. These three D’s summarize the primary functional disturbances: dyskinesia is the biomechanical aspect (abnormal motion or alignment of a joint), dysafferentation is the neurological afferent aspect (altered sensory input to the CNS), and dysautonomia is the resulting autonomic imbalance that can occur (sympathetic/parasympathetic dysregulation)
irispublishers.com
irispublishers.com
. In a clinical sense, Kent encourages practitioners to assess subluxations not just by feeling for a bone out of place, but by looking for evidence of these dysfunctions – e.g. altered posture or gait (dyskinesia), sensorimotor testing like joint position sense or balance (to infer dysafferentation), and autonomic measures like pulse or heart rate variability (dysautonomia). If present, these can be tracked as indicators of subluxation and of improvement after care
irispublishers.com
. Kent also reintroduced the term dysponesis into the lexicon (originally used by psychologist Dr. Lowell Ward in the 1970s) to describe the habitual, subliminal misdirected neural reactions to various stimuli – essentially the neurophysiological stress response that can result in a constellation of subluxations. He and others in the CLA group have developed technologies (Surface EMG, thermal scans, HRV monitors) to try to measure these components in chiropractic offices, reflecting the drive to objectify the subluxation. Interdisciplinary Acknowledgement: Outside of chiropractic, there is growing recognition of the same phenomenon under different names. Mainstream manual therapy talks of “joint dysfunction,” “segmental joint restriction,” or “somatic dysfunction”, which echo the chiropractic subluxation (minus the historical baggage). For example, orthopedic researcher Panjabi in 2006 described how a loss of spinal segmental stability (after injury or strain) can lead to “disturbed kinematics, loss of spatial and temporal integrity of afferent receptor signals, and corrupted motor programs.”
chiro.org
 This is essentially dyskinesia plus dysafferentation in scientific parlance. The World Health Organization now defines a chiropractic subluxation as a “dysfunction in a joint or motion segment in which alignment, movement integrity and/or physiological function are altered, although contact between joint surfaces remains intact.”
en.wikipedia.org
 – notably emphasizing functional change, not gross dislocation. This is in line with the modern consensus that the “subluxation” is a benign but impactful lesion, detectable by its functional impairments and neurological sequelae, rather than by visible displacements on imaging
en.wikipedia.org
.
Conclusion
Over the past 125 years, the concept of vertebral subluxation has evolved from a simple “bone out of place” theory into a sophisticated multidimensional model of neurophysiological dysfunction. Early pioneers like D.D. and B.J. Palmer laid a philosophical foundation, highlighting the importance of nerve tone and the triad of physical, chemical, and emotional stress in disrupting the nervous system
drannejensen.com
pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov
. Mid-century clinicians such as Janse and Gillet brought focus to joint biomechanics – emphasizing fixation and loss of normal motion as key instigators of neurologic impairment
chiro.org
chiro.org
. By integrating insights from osteopathy and neurology (Korr’s facilitation, Denslow’s reflexes), chiropractors in the 1970s–90s reframed subluxation as a “Vertebral Subluxation Complex,” comprising inflammatory, muscular, neural, and biomechanical components
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Thematic hypotheses emerged: proprioceptive insult (dysafferentation causing sensorimotor glitches)
chiro.org
chiro.org
, reflex muscle splinting (gamma loop fixation), autonomic imbalance (sympathetic overdrive linking to organ dysfunction)
chiro.org
chiro.org
, and neurodystrophy (immune and tissue changes from chronic neural stress)
chiro.org
. Contemporary researchers like Haavik and Kent have synthesized these ideas with modern neuroscience, describing subluxation as a central motor control problem with maladaptive neural plasticity – in essence, a learned neuro-spinal dysfunction that both reflects and reinforces the body’s response to stress
ncmicfoundation.org
. The subluxation can be seen as the juncture where stress physiology meets spinal pathomechanics: for example, prolonged emotional stress (thoughts) might increase muscle tension in the neck, leading to joint strain and dyskinesia; or a toxic inflammatory diet might sensitize neurons such that a minor injury leads to exaggerated pain and facilitation. These scenarios result in a common end-point – a dysfunctional vertebral segment with complex neurological reverberations. In clinical practice, the goal of chiropractic care has correspondingly broadened. No longer is the adjustment aimed solely at “putting a bone back in place,” but rather at restoring normal joint motion, normalizing afferent signals, and rebalancing nervous system tone
chiro.org
. By unlocking a fixated segment, the adjustment reduces mechanoreceptor and nociceptor aberration, quiets down the segmental reflex circuitry, and can even down-regulate excessive sympathetic output
chiro.org
chiro.org
. This in turn may alleviate pain, relax muscle splinting, improve proprioception and coordination, and allow the body’s stress response to recalibrate. The cumulative effect is to free the body’s self-healing and regulatory capacity – echoing D.D. Palmer’s original premise of allowing “Innate Intelligence” (biological wisdom) to better express itself. In summary, vertebral subluxation is best understood as a dynamic neurobiological process: it develops when stresses – be they physical forces, biochemical imbalances, or emotional strains – push a spinal joint beyond its adaptive capacity, causing a cascade of changes (inflammation, muscle guarding, altered nerve firing) that can entrench the joint in a state of dysfunction. This state is characterized by local findings (pain, tension, restricted mobility) and corroborated by neurological signs (altered reflexes, autonomic changes, proprioceptive deficits). Over time, if uncorrected, it may contribute to degenerative changes or functional health issues, as multiple body systems adapt to the distorted neural signals
chiro.org
chiro.org
. Chiropractic and related manual therapies seek to intervene in this process – reducing the physical dysfunction and its neurological impact. Decades of interdisciplinary research now provide a plausible scientific basis for each aspect of subluxation. Far from being a mythical “bone out of place,” the subluxation can be viewed as a real, albeit complex, pathophysiological entity – essentially the chiropractic term for a common condition also recognized in osteopathy, physiotherapy, and neurology: the dysfunctional spinal segment with its myriad reflex effects
apcj.net
chiro.org
. Today’s definitive models of subluxation integrate the historical wisdom with modern evidence. They show that whether the cause is a trauma (physical injury), a toxin (chemical/inflammatory stress), or a thought (emotional stress), the end result on the spine and nervous system can be understood in terms of segmental facilitation, dysafferentation, and dysautonomia – and importantly, these are potentially reversible. As research continues, chiropractic’s focus on the spine-neuroimmune connection is increasingly resonant with cutting-edge neuroscience. The hope of early chiropractors that adjusting subluxations could restore health may find its explanation in the normalization of central neural circuits and stress responses. In the words of D.D. Palmer, “I will keep refining this science… for I know that the cause of disease lies in the nervous system.” Over a century later, we see that many of those early clinical insights have been validated and refined by neurophysiological models of subluxation
chiro.org
chiro.org
 – truly a testament to an evolving understanding of the body’s intricate interplay between the spine and the nervous system. Sources:

2.4 Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic and the Emergence of OsseoTonal Techniques
As tonal awareness deepened within the chiropractic profession throughout the 20th and 21st centuries, multiple techniques contributed foundational insights that expanded the understanding of subluxation beyond the vertebral model. These contributions shaped the path toward a tonal paradigm—where subluxation is seen not merely as mechanical fixation or misalignment, but as a state of altered tone and disrupted neurological coherence.
Simultaneously, a new category of chiropractic technique emerged—blending structural contact with tonal awareness. These techniques engage the nervous system through joint articulations but are directed by tonal indicators and intent. They are collectively referred to as OsseoTonal techniques: an emerging class of approaches that integrate osseous (articular) contact with neurologically driven tonal analysis and correction strategies.
This section outlines two distinct categories:
The foundational contributions to tonal chiropractic, including early tonal awarenesses and reflexive analysis tools
A growing collection of OsseoTonal techniques, which reflect the profession’s evolving synthesis of structure and tone.
Foundational Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic
The following techniques represent pivotal contributions to the emergence and refinement of tonal chiropractic within the profession.
Logan Basic Technique: Pioneered in 1931 by Dr. H.B. Logan, this technique recognized subtle shifts in postural tone and sought to influence global alignment through light, sustained sacral contacts. Its primary contact point—the sacrotuberous ligament—affects NeuroSpinal tone directly via its connective tissue and dural attachments to the C-SMFU. Though developed decades before the term “tonal” was widely used, Logan Basic stands as an early example of non-articular tonal engagement, influencing the tone of the NeuroSpinal system without joint manipulation.
(Logan University, https://www.logan.edu)

Directional Non-Force Technique (DNFT): Established as a seminar series in the 1940’s by Dr. Richard Van Rumpt, DNFT introduced the chiropractic profession to pressure testing and neurological leg checks as reflexive indicators for analysis and post-adjustment verification. While DNFT was not developed as a tonal technique, its use of the body’s own feedback to guide the adjustment laid a critical foundation for future tonal analysis models.
(DNFT Chiropractic, https://www.dnftchiropractic.com)

Upper Cervical Chiropractic: Pioneered by B.J. Palmer in the 1930s, Upper Cervical Chiropractic emphasized that misalignments in the upper cervical spine could have widespread effects on the nervous system. Later, John D. Grostic’s Dentate Ligament–Cord Distortion Hypothesis (1988) provided a biomechanical basis, demonstrating how such misalignments might induce global dural tension through mechanical stress on the spinal cord via the dentate ligaments. This early recognition of global neurological effects from localized structural disturbances helped lay the conceptual groundwork for tonal chiropractic, reinforcing the principle that precise input can influence systemic tone and adaptive function.
(Palmer, 1934; Grostic, 1988)

Toftness Technique (1950s): Developed the first entirely tonal approach, using electromagnetic field detection to locate interference patterns in the nervous system
(Toftness Postgraduate School of Chiropractic, https://www.toftness.org)

Thompson Technique (1955): Expanded the use of Van Rumpt’s leg checks. Several reflexive indicators identified by Thompson have been reframed through the lens of global tone in TTC
(Thompson Technique Foundation, https://www.thompsontechnique.org; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Pierce-Stillwagon Technique (PST) (1963): Developed by Dr. Walter Vernon Pierce, Sr. and Dr. Glenn Stillwagon, the Pierce-Stillwagon Technique emerged in 1963 as a refinement and synthesis of earlier chiropractic approaches, including Palmer HIO, Thompson, Logan Basic, and the Reaver system. PST introduced a biomechanical analysis of spinal motion using “stress views” (flexion, extension, rotation, and lateral bending) and later, videofluoroscopy, to assess functional loss within the spinal system—placing motion as a central indicator of vertebral subluxation. The technique incorporated neurological evaluation tools such as palpation, leg checks, and thermographic instrumentation to guide specific, segmental corrections.

While PST was primarily structural in its application, its analytical system contributed foundational components to later techniques, including early frameworks for Torque Release Technique (TRT). Following Dr. Pierce’s passing in 1993, the technique evolved into what is now known as the Pierce Results System (PRS), continuing to influence analysis protocols in modern chiropractic.
(PRS Chiropractic, https://prschiropractic.com/about-the-system/what-is-the-pierce-results-system; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Network Chiropractic (1987): Created by Dr. Donald Epstein, Network Chiropractic—now known as Network Spinal Analysis (NSA)—expanded the tonal paradigm through a synthesis of indicators from multiple pre-existing techniques into a comprehensive tonal analysis system, elements of which continue to be utilized within the TTC model. A distinguishing feature of NSA is its emphasis on the expression of two unique wave phenomena: the respiratory wave and the somatopsychic wave, which reflect enhanced neural organization and mind-body integration. The contacts in NSA are oriented with an awareness of these wave propagations, aiming to cue the nervous system toward greater coherence, adaptability, and self-regulation through gentle spinal contacts at specific gateways.
(EpiEnergetics, https://www.epienergetics.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Torque Release Technique (TRT) (1995): Developed by Dr. Marvin Talsky and co-founded by Dr. Jay Holder, TRT introduced an instrument-assisted tonal approach using the Integrator™. A key distinction in TRT’s evolutionary uniqueness was its incorporation of tonal aspects from all of the aforementioned techniques, with a particular dependency on Van Rumpt’s pressure testing and leg checks—applied, however, with tonal awareness, intent, and application. While rooted in tonal philosophy, Dr. Talsky later recognized that many of TRT’s core applications remained grounded in a Newtonian, mechanically-oriented model—what we now term an OsseoTonal approach, to distinguish it from fully tonal systems. It was considered a tonal technique until new discoveries revealed that directly engaging the NeuroSpinal system through non-articular means defines a truly tonal approach—while an OsseoTonal approach engages the NeuroSpinal system indirectly via osseous (articular) adjustments. This realization prompted the development of Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) as a distinct tonal paradigm aligned with vitalistic principles and a direct engagement with the tone of the NeuroSpinal system.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Bio-Geometric Integration (BGI): Established as a seminar series in 1995 by Dr. Sue Brown, Bio-Geometric Integration (BGI) integrates concepts from quantum physics, tonal philosophy, and sacred geometry into chiropractic analysis and adjusting. A distinctive feature of BGI is its emphasis on the body’s biotensegrity and the propagation of tonal wave dynamics throughout the entire system.
Adjustments in BGI are guided by the innate geometry of the body, with vectors oriented to global structural patterns and wave dynamics—rather than to specific lines of aberrant tension as in TTC. Instead of utilizing pressure testing or leg checks to identify subluxation focal points, BGI practitioners rely on tonal palpation and subtle energetic indicators to determine when, where, and how to engage. This approach emphasizes the integration and release of stored potential energy, facilitating healing and evolution by restoring coherence within the body’s natural geometric design.
(BGI HQ, https://www.bgihq.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC): Established as a seminar series in 2001 by Dr. Marvin Talsky, TTC emerged as a fully tonal paradigm that integrates the analytical strengths and tonal insights of earlier techniques while introducing a distinct model centered on the NeuroSpinal system. TTC identifies and engages aberrant tone within the Cranio-Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit directly—without reliance on joint articulation—using specific vectors aligned with the lines of NeuroSpinal tension themselves. Unlike earlier tonal approaches that engage the NeuroSpinal system through articular contacts, wave propagation, or global geometry, TTC delivers the least amount of the most effective input in the precise direction of NeuroSpinal unwinding. A defining feature of TTC is its reliance on tonal pressure testing and neurological leg checks to verify that the chiropractor is facilitating an adjustment through the absolute best window into the NeuroSpinal system. This clarity of analysis, intent, and application distinguishes TTC as the first chiropractic system to engage the tone of the NeuroSpinal system directly as its primary objective.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Access Workshop: Developed in 2002 by Dr. Ric Wiegand, Access Workshop offers a unique contribution to tonal chiropractic through its emphasis on non-linear analysis and interoceptive awareness. Rooted in contemporary neuroscience, Access trains chiropractors to recognize subtle internal cues from their own central autonomic network (CAN) as a means of identifying areas of neural disruption within the patient. Rather than relying on structural findings or externally observed patterns, the Access approach cultivates the chiropractor’s ability to detect the body’s internal “alert response”—a subtle, reflexive signal indicating an area of neurological significance. This method enhances the practitioner’s ability to deliver precise, appropriately scaled input by matching the system’s state of receptivity, thereby refining tonal discernment and clinical impact within a wide range of techniques.
(Access Workshop, https://www.accessworkshop.com)

OsseoTonal Techniques in Modern Chiropractic

As the tonal chiropractic paradigm continues to evolve, a growing number of techniques have emerged that blend tonal awareness with structural (osseous) application. These OsseoTonal approaches engage the NeuroSpinal system through articular contacts and biomechanical vectors, but do so with an intentional tonal framework—emphasizing presence, coherence, and vibrational dynamics in the adjusting process. While they do not directly engage the tone of the NeuroSpinal system in the manner of TTC, these emerging techniques reflect the profession’s increasing integration of tonal principles into structurally-oriented methods.

Mastery, Love, Service (MLS): Developed in 1984 by Dr. Arno Burnier, MLS Seminars emerged from his desire to cultivate a chiropractic approach that emphasizes tone, presence, service, and connection during adjustments. The technique focuses on bringing the nervous system to a state of “physiological peace and ease,” highlighting the importance of the chiropractor’s intent and the energetic exchange between practitioner and patient. A distinguishing feature of MLS is its reverence for the body’s innate intelligence and the tone of the NeuroSpinal system, guiding chiropractors in determining when, where, and how to deliver an osseous adjustment with precision and respect. This approach fosters a deeper connection to the art of chiropractic, transforming adjustments into honoring, specific, fluid, and powerful exchanges.
(MLS Seminars, https://www.mlsseminars.com)

MC2 Technique (1996): Developed by Dr. Steve Hoffman, MC2 builds upon principles from Torque Release Technique (TRT) but applies them within a tonal model focused on nervous system balance, particularly at the upper cervical level. MC2 introduced a system of biotonal analysis and reflexive indicators to determine the most efficient and neurologically responsive adjustment. Its development emphasized the tonal correction of the nervous system first, followed by secondary postural and structural effects.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025; Chiropractic Mastery, https://www.chiropracticmastery.com)

Tonal Integrative Correction (TIC) (2010s): Created by Dr. Jason Sabo, TIC integrates structural and tonal systems with specific attention to meningeal, osseous, and postural models, emphasizing harmonic resonance (Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025; TIC Chiropractic, https://www.ticchiro.com)

Kairos Training Culture (KTC) (2016): Emphasizes the art and mastery of adjusting with an emphasis on biomechanics, tone, and flow. KTC integrates both tonal awareness and precise osseous delivery  (Kairos Training Culture, https://www.kairostrainingculture.com) 
Syntropy Chiropractic Training (2020s): Focuses on adjusting mastery with seminars and digital resources that prioritize flow state and precision, combining tonal and structural elements (Syntropy Chiropractic, https://www.syntropychirotraining.com) 
Pneuma Chiropractic (2020s): Synthesizes multiple healing traditions and emphasizes tonal, neural, meningeal, and energetic awareness, with adjusting procedures that reflect tonal integration (Pneuma Chiropractic, https://www.pneumachiropractic.com)

2.4 Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic and the Emergence of OsseoTonal Techniques
As tonal awareness deepened within the chiropractic profession throughout the 20th and 21st centuries, multiple techniques contributed foundational insights that expanded the understanding of subluxation beyond the vertebral model. These contributions shaped the path toward a tonal paradigm—where subluxation is seen not merely as mechanical fixation or misalignment, but as a state of altered tone and disrupted neurological coherence.

Simultaneously, a new category of chiropractic technique emerged—blending structural contact with tonal awareness. These techniques engage the nervous system through joint articulations but are directed by tonal indicators and intent. They are collectively referred to as OsseoTonal techniques: an emerging class of approaches that integrate osseous (articular) contact with neurologically driven tonal analysis and correction strategies.

This section outlines two distinct categories:

Foundational contributions to tonal chiropractic, including early tonal awareness and reflexive analysis tools

A growing collection of OsseoTonal techniques, which reflect the profession’s evolving synthesis of structure and tone

2.4a Foundational Contributions to Tonal Chiropractic
Logan Basic Technique: Pioneered in 1931 by Dr. H.B. Logan, this technique recognized subtle shifts in postural tone and sought to influence global alignment through light, sustained sacral contacts. Its primary contact point—the sacrotuberous ligament—affects NeuroSpinal tone directly via its connective tissue and dural attachments to the C-SMFU. Though developed decades before the term “tonal” was widely used, Logan Basic stands as an early example of non-articular tonal engagement, influencing the tone of the NeuroSpinal system without joint manipulation.
(Logan University, https://www.logan.edu)

Directional Non-Force Technique (DNFT): Established as a seminar series in the 1940s by Dr. Richard Van Rumpt, DNFT introduced the chiropractic profession to pressure testing and neurological leg checks as reflexive indicators for analysis and post-adjustment verification. While DNFT was not developed as a tonal technique, its use of the body’s own feedback to guide the adjustment laid a critical foundation for future tonal analysis models.
(DNFT Chiropractic, https://www.dnftchiropractic.com)

Upper Cervical Chiropractic: Pioneered by B.J. Palmer in the 1930s, Upper Cervical Chiropractic emphasized that misalignments in the upper cervical spine could have widespread effects on the nervous system. Later, John D. Grostic’s Dentate Ligament–Cord Distortion Hypothesis (1988) provided a biomechanical basis, demonstrating how such misalignments might induce global dural tension through mechanical stress on the spinal cord via the dentate ligaments. This early recognition of global neurological effects from localized structural disturbances helped lay the conceptual groundwork for tonal chiropractic, reinforcing the principle that precise input can influence systemic tone and adaptive function.
(Palmer, 1934; Grostic, 1988)

Toftness Technique: Developed in the 1950s by Dr. I.N. Toftness, this was the first chiropractic approach to abandon osseous analysis entirely, using electromagnetic field detection to locate areas of interference in the nervous system. It remains one of the earliest true tonal techniques.
(Toftness Postgraduate School of Chiropractic, https://www.toftness.org)

Thompson Technique (1955): While primarily structural, Thompson expanded DNFT’s leg checks and introduced reflex-based indicators that would later be reinterpreted through the lens of NeuroSpinal tone within TTC.
(Thompson Technique Foundation, https://www.thompsontechnique.org; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Pierce-Stillwagon Technique (1963): Developed by Dr. Walter Vernon Pierce, Sr. and Dr. Glenn Stillwagon, this technique emerged as a refinement and synthesis of earlier chiropractic approaches. Though biomechanical in its application, Pierce-Stillwagon’s emphasis on instrumentation, stress views, and segmental motion loss contributed key analytic methods that informed later tonal and OsseoTonal systems.
(PRS Chiropractic, https://prschiropractic.com)

Network Chiropractic (1987): Created by Dr. Donald Epstein, Network Chiropractic—now known as Network Spinal Analysis (NSA)—expanded the tonal paradigm through a synthesis of indicators from multiple pre-existing techniques into a comprehensive tonal analysis system, elements of which continue to be utilized within the TTC model. A distinguishing feature of NSA is its emphasis on the expression of two unique wave phenomena: the respiratory wave and the somatopsychic wave, which reflect enhanced neural organization and mind-body integration. The contacts in NSA are oriented with an awareness of these wave propagations, aiming to cue the nervous system toward greater coherence, adaptability, and self-regulation through gentle spinal contacts at specific gateways.
(EpiEnergetics, https://www.epienergetics.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Torque Release Technique (1995): Developed by Dr. Marvin Talsky and co-founded by Dr. Jay Holder, TRT introduced an instrument-assisted tonal approach using the Integrator™. Although rooted in tonal philosophy, TRT continued to engage the NeuroSpinal system through osseous articulation—placing it within the OsseoTonal category.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (2001): Founded by Dr. Marvin Talsky, TTC emerged as the first chiropractic technique to fully engage the NeuroSpinal system directly—without reliance on joint articulation. TTC uses tonal indicators, leg checks, and precise non-articular contact to communicate corrective input in the vector of unwinding. It delivers the least amount of the most effective input at the best window in, allowing the body to reinitiate self-adjustment from within.
(Talsky & Nadler, TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

Access Workshop (2002): Developed by Dr. Ric Wiegand, Access Workshop emphasizes non-linear tonal analysis by training the chiropractor’s own central autonomic network to detect subtle, reflexive alerts of tension within the patient.
(Access Workshop, https://www.accessworkshop.com)

CLA Insight Technology: Introduced in the 1990s by the Chiropractic Leadership Alliance (CLA), Insight scanning technology provides non-invasive, reproducible neurophysiological data including thermal scans, surface electromyography (sEMG), and heart rate variability (HRV). These measurements offer a tonal window into autonomic balance, muscular symmetry, and stress adaptability—supporting analysis strategies that align with tonal chiropractic objectives.
(Chiropractic Leadership Alliance, https://insightcla.com)

2.4b OsseoTonal Techniques in Modern Chiropractic
As the tonal chiropractic paradigm continues to evolve, a growing number of techniques have emerged that blend tonal awareness with structural (osseous) application. These OsseoTonal approaches engage the NeuroSpinal system through articular contacts and biomechanical vectors, but do so with an intentional tonal framework—emphasizing presence, coherence, and vibrational dynamics in the adjusting process. While they do not directly engage the tone of the NeuroSpinal system in the manner of TTC, these emerging techniques reflect the profession’s increasing integration of tonal principles into structurally oriented methods.

Mastery, Love, Service (MLS): Developed in 1984 by Dr. Arno Burnier, this technique emphasizes intent, presence, and vibrational resonance within osseous adjustments to bring the nervous system into a state of “physiological peace and ease.”
(MLS Seminars, https://www.mlsseminars.com)

MC2 Technique (1996): Developed by Dr. Steve Hoffman, MC2 builds on TRT principles and focuses on biotonal balance—particularly within the upper cervical region—using reflexive indicators to guide care.
(Chiropractic Mastery, https://www.chiropracticmastery.com)

Tonal Integrative Correction (TIC): Created by Dr. Jason Sabo, TIC integrates tonal, meningeal, and osseous models through harmonic resonance and leg-check indicators.
(TIC Chiropractic, https://www.ticchiro.com)

Kairos Training Culture (KTC): Launched in 2016, KTC emphasizes flow state, biomechanics, and tonal awareness within high-level osseous adjusting.
(Kairos Training Culture, https://www.kairostrainingculture.com)

Syntropy Chiropractic Training: Focused on precision, coherence, and rhythm in osseous adjusting, integrating tonal principles into advanced technique refinement.
(Syntropy Chiropractic, https://www.syntropychirotraining.com)

Pneuma Chiropractic: Synthesizes neural, energetic, and tonal models into adjusting protocols that blend awareness of the C-SMFU with vibrational pattern recognition.
(Pneuma Chiropractic, https://www.pneumachiropractic.com)

Synthesis:
Taken together, these foundational techniques and emerging OsseoTonal innovations illustrate a continuous evolution—from mechanical correction to tonal engagement. Each step contributed toward the development of a chiropractic model that recognizes tone as the language of adaptation, and the NeuroSpinal system as the medium of communication. Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC) builds on this legacy, clarifying and distilling the tonal paradigm into a reproducible protocol that prioritizes the least amount of the most effective input, delivered at the best window in—aligning precisely with the direction of NeuroSpinal unwinding. TTC honors and transcends its predecessors, offering a uniquely direct engagement with the core mechanism of subluxation: aberrant tone within the NeuroSpinal system.

The unique contribution of the Pierce-Stillwagon Technique (PST) to the emergence and evolution of tonal chiropractic lies in the following key areas:

🔷 1. Stress View Analysis as a Precursor to Tonal Indicators
PST was one of the first techniques to systematically use “stress views” (flexion, extension, rotation, and lateral bending) to evaluate the spine under movement-based load—not just in neutral static positioning. This focus on functional adaptability under stress prefigured a tonal understanding of the spine—not merely as bones misaligned, but as a system responding dynamically to physical stress.

➤ This helped shift the analysis paradigm from “what’s out of place” to “where is the system unable to adapt?”—a tonal question.

🔷 2. Thermographic and Neurological Correlation
PST incorporated instrumentation like thermography to track neurological asymmetries over time, linking structural findings with ongoing changes in nervous system function. This was an early attempt to track neurophysiological coherence—a central concern of tonal models.

➤ It framed structural distortions as potentially downstream from neurological signaling issues, even if not explicitly described in tonal terms at the time.

🔷 3. Segmental Motion and Neurological Feedback
While PST remained largely structural in application, its emphasis on motion loss and neurological evaluation tools (e.g., leg checks, reflex indicators) reflected a growing interest in the relationship between movement, tone, and neural feedback.

➤ This bridge between segmental biomechanics and nervous system analysis primed the profession for the tonal shift, where tone—not fixation—is primary.

🔷 4. Legacy Influence on Later Tonal Systems
The Pierce Results System (PRS), the evolution of PST after Dr. Pierce’s passing, directly influenced the development of Torque Release Technique (TRT)—which in turn led to the formation of Talsky Tonal Chiropractic (TTC). Thus, PST plays an indirect but important ancestral role in the genealogy of tonal techniques.

✍️ Summary Wording for Your White Paper:
While primarily structural in application, Pierce-Stillwagon Technique contributed foundational elements to the tonal chiropractic paradigm through its focus on stress-response views, thermographic correlation with neurological activity, and motion-based segmental evaluation. These analytic innovations helped set the stage for techniques like TRT and TTC by emphasizing the functional adaptability of the spinal system—not just static alignment—marking an early transition from positional to tonal thinking.

The term "Cervical Syndrome" was notably explored by Dr. Ruth Jackson, MD, FACS, in her seminal work titled The Cervical Syndrome. Dr. Jackson was a pioneering figure in spinal medicine and the first female orthopedic surgeon in the United States. Her contributions significantly advanced the understanding of cervical spine disorders and their clinical implications. 
PubMed Central

In the realm of diagnostic testing, the Spurling Test, also known as Spurling's neck compression test, was introduced by neurosurgeons Roy Glen Spurling and William Beecher Scoville. This test is designed to assess cervical radiculopathy by applying pressure to the head while the neck is extended and rotated, potentially eliciting nerve root symptoms.

The assessment you're referring to—where a patient lies prone, and turning their head causes a functional short leg to balance—is commonly known in chiropractic practice as the Cervical Syndrome Test. This test is often associated with the Derifield-Thompson leg check analysis, a component of the Thompson Technique.
PubMed
+1
Logan University
+1

In this procedure, the chiropractor observes the patient's leg lengths while the patient is lying face down. If a functional leg length discrepancy is present, turning the head to one side may cause the legs to appear even. This response is interpreted as an indicator of cervical involvement, particularly at the C1 or C5 vertebral levels.

The Derifield-Thompson leg check was developed by Dr. J. Clay Thompson as part of the Thompson Technique, which he introduced in the 1950s. This method utilizes leg length analysis to detect subluxations and determine the appropriate adjustments needed.

You said:
did PST come up with any unique indicators
ChatGPT said:
Yes, the Pierce-Stillwagon Technique (PST) did introduce several unique indicators and analytic innovations that were influential—particularly in bridging structural analysis with emerging tonal awareness. Here are the most notable contributions:

🔷 1. Stress View Radiographic Analysis
PST pioneered the systematic use of "stress views"—dynamic X-ray imaging in flexion, extension, rotation, and lateral bending—to assess where motion was lost under load. This was a unique functional indicator not commonly used in chiropractic at the time. Instead of relying on static misalignments, PST used this dynamic data to infer functional adaptation and stress patterns in the spine.

Impact: This laid the groundwork for recognizing how spinal structures respond to load, stress, and compensation, a concept that resonated later with tonal models like TTC which view subluxation as the body’s response to stress rather than a static misalignment.

🔷 2. Videofluoroscopy (VFC)
Dr. Pierce was one of the earliest chiropractors to adopt and refine videofluoroscopy—real-time motion X-ray imaging—to analyze intersegmental motion in the spine. This allowed for:

Precise identification of hypomobile segments

Visualization of “hard-end feel” movement dysfunctions

Inference of mechanical stress points in the kinetic chain

Impact: While highly structural, this emphasis on visualizing and measuring dynamic spinal behavior anticipated later tonal paradigms, which prioritize functional adaptability and coherent motion as indicators of health.

🔷 3. Pattern Analysis through Thermography
PST also integrated thermographic scanning using paraspinal temperature readings to detect patterns of neurological disturbance and autonomic imbalance. Although not exclusive to PST, its consistent use in combination with structural data (X-ray, palpation, leg checks) was part of its analytic uniqueness.

Impact: This attempt to correlate neurological findings with structural data helped shift chiropractic thinking toward pattern recognition, a core feature in many tonal systems today.

🔷 4. Segmental Neurological Indicators
Although not as widely recognized as DNFT-style reflex testing, PST used a form of segmental palpation and neurological muscle response testing that identified motor-sensory feedback distortions at specific spinal levels. This included:

Localized paraspinal muscle tone changes

Postural shifts under specific loading conditions

Segment-specific palpatory findings

Impact: These indicators helped bridge the gap between segmental dysfunction and neurological consequence, an important precursor to the fully integrated tonal analyses of TTC.

✅ Summary:
While PST was primarily structural, its stress view imaging, early videofluoroscopy, neurological correlation via thermography, and pattern-based thinking were uniquely innovative for its time and contributed important building blocks to the tonal chiropractic evolution.

TTC builds upon this by replacing joint-centric indicators with tonal indicators, but PST’s legacy remains in its systematic analysis of functional dysfunction—not just static misalignment.

Koren Specific Technique (KST) came first.

Access Workshop was developed by Dr. Ric Wiegand in 2002, after KST had already begun to circulate in the profession.
