KST preceded Access Workshop.

Applied Kinesiology (AK): Introduced by Dr. George Goodheart in 1964, Applied Kinesiology (AK) brought manual muscle testing into chiropractic as a diagnostic and therapeutic tool. While not a tonal technique per se, AK contributed significantly to the evolution of tonal analysis by establishing muscle testing as a method of engaging in a “neurological conversation” with the body—similar in principle to the use of pressure testing and leg checks.

This broader concept of “asking the body” aligns with the nonlinear assessment strategies of tonal chiropractic, in contrast to the static, linear logic of motion palpation used in traditional osseous techniques. Many techniques have since evolved from AK’s foundational model, reinforcing the idea that the nervous system can be queried directly for real-time feedback and precision-guided input.

(ICAK, https://www.icakusa.com; TTC Seminar Notes, 2025)

ChatGPT said:
Sacro Occipital Technique (SOT) was developed by Dr. Major Bertrand DeJarnette, D.O., D.C., beginning in 1925, following his graduation from the Nebraska College of Chiropractic in 1924. DeJarnette's extensive research and clinical observations led to the formalization of SOT by 1929. His work integrated concepts from osteopathy, chiropractic, and engineering, focusing on the relationship between the sacrum and occiput, cerebrospinal fluid dynamics, and meningeal tension. SOT's development introduced a system of categorizing patients based on specific patterns of imbalance, allowing for tailored adjustments aimed at restoring neurological function and structural integrity. DeJarnette continued to refine SOT throughout his career, contributing significantly to the evolution of tonal chiropractic approaches.

Dr. Reggie Gold's influence in the chiropractic profession began shortly after his graduation from Palmer School of Chiropractic in 1957. He quickly became a prominent figure, known for his compelling lectures and dedication to chiropractic philosophy. In the 1970s, he was instrumental in the establishment of Sherman College of Straight Chiropractic and served as its first president. His teachings and writings, including "The Triune of Life," have been foundational in chiropractic education. Dr. Gold's commitment to the profession continued throughout his life, and he remained an influential voice until his passing in 2012.

D.D. Palmer, Founder of Chiropractic: D.D. Palmer’s original vision laid the philosophical foundation for tonal chiropractic. He asserted that “life is an expression of tone,” and defined disease as “a variation in tone”—referring to the vibratory frequency of nerves, tissues, and the body's energy systems (Palmer, 1910, The Chiropractor’s Adjuster). Though limited by the anatomical understanding of his time, Palmer clearly identified the role of nervous system tone in both health and dysfunction. His concept of Innate Intelligence guiding bodily function and his recognition that subluxation interferes with this expression set the stage for a tone-first approach to chiropractic (Palmer, 1906, The Science, Art and Philosophy of Chiropractic).

Palmer’s original paradigm was not strictly structural or mechanical. It was vitalistic and rooted in the idea that intelligence flows through matter via tone. Tonal chiropractic extends and deepens this vision, shifting emphasis from bone misalignment to functional coherence within the NeuroSpinal system.

Sacro Occipital Technique (SOT): Developed by Dr. M.B. DeJarnette in the 1920s, SOT brought forward a systems-based model that linked cranial, pelvic, and spinal dynamics through dural and cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) flow. By emphasizing meningeal tension patterns, CSF rhythm, and dural release, SOT highlighted the functional interdependence of the cranium and sacrum—connected through the dural membranes of the spinal cord. While not considered a tonal technique by its original design, SOT laid foundational groundwork for tonal chiropractic’s emergence by addressing dural tone and its relationship to global physiological function. Its Category System, cranial work, and pelvic blocking strategies continue to inform tonal models today.

Sacro Occipital Technique (SOT): Developed by Dr. Major Bertrand DeJarnette in the late 1920s, SOT is a chiropractic technique that focuses on the relationship between the sacrum and occiput. It emphasizes the role of the dural system and cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) dynamics in maintaining neurological function. SOT categorizes patients into three groups to identify specific patterns of imbalance and employs methods such as pelvic blocking and cranial adjustments to address these issues. By targeting the dural tension and promoting optimal CSF flow, SOT aims to restore balance and function within the nervous system. This approach has laid foundational groundwork for the development of tonal chiropractic models.

Based on the search of The Dynamics of Spinal Stress (1980) by Dr. Lowell E. Ward, here are the most relevant insights into his contributions that directly influenced what would evolve into the NeuroSpinal System concept foundational to Tonal Chiropractic:

✅ Key Contributions by Lowell E. Ward to the Evolution of Tonal Chiropractic:
Unitized Functional Model of the Spine:
Ward consistently emphasized the spine as a "singular, synchronous, functional unit" rather than a collection of individual vertebrae. He challenged the prevailing sectional analysis by treating the spinal column and pelvis as a continuous, structurally and neurologically integrated system
.

This parallels the TTC concept of the Cranio-Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C-SMFU), and can be seen as a precursor to its development.

Spinal Cord and Meningeal Stress Fixations:
Ward introduced the idea that meningeal tension—particularly in the spinal cord's dural structures—plays a key role in subluxation, often indicated by phenomena like the functional short leg. He states that:

“The spinal cord meningeal stress fixation is probably one of the foremost components of the spinal fixation and fixed leg deficiencies.”

Spinal Column Stressology:
Ward developed and named Spinal Column Stressology—a diagnostic and treatment model based on quantifying spinal stress and compensatory patterns using large-format spinal stress x-rays and specialized nomographs to predict disease processes
.

Spinal Synchronization and Pattern-Based Thinking:
Ward proposed a pattern-based model of compensation, where distortions in the spine followed predictable paths due to stress overload. This aligns philosophically with tonal chiropractic's system-based, pattern-recognition approach to interference.

Mind-Body and Stress Linkage:
His system emphasized the emotional and psychological origins of stress and their direct impact on structural integrity, which supports the allostatic load model used in tonal theory and TTC:

“The clinical practice of spinal stressology experiences an incessant interaction between orthopedic stress and psychological distress.”

Early Description of Dural and Cord Tension as a Root Cause:
Ward and Breig (1978) both emphasized tension-based interference in the spinal cord and dura. These writings established the idea that neurological distortion can exist without bone misalignment—later reinterpreted in TTC as aberrant NeuroSpinal tone
.

Lowell E. Ward, D.C. (1980): Through his model of Spinal Column Stressology, Dr. Lowell Ward reframed the spine as a singular, synchronized, functional unit rather than a segmented biomechanical structure. His analysis emphasized that dural and spinal cord tension—not just vertebral misalignment—are key contributors to subluxation. Ward’s documentation of meningeal stress fixations, particularly in relation to functional leg-length discrepancy, foreshadowed the modern concept of the Cranio-Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C-SMFU). His work forms a critical anatomical and physiological bridge to the emergence of the NeuroSpinal model foundational to Talsky Tonal Chiropractic.
(Ward, The Dynamics of Spinal Stress, 1980)

ChatGPT said:
Alf Breig’s Adverse Mechanical Tension in the Central Nervous System (1978) provides foundational insight that significantly influenced the emergence of a tonal chiropractic paradigm—especially in its redefinition of interference as stemming from tension rather than compression.

✅ Key Contributions from Alf Breig Relevant to Tonal Chiropractic:
1. Mechanical Tension Affects CNS Without Vertebral Displacement
Breig demonstrated that adverse mechanical tension in the spinal cord and meninges can cause neurological dysfunction even in the absence of direct vertebral compression. This helped shift the model of interference from "bone-on-nerve" compression to tension-based models of dysfunction
.

2. The Spinal Cord and Meninges Operate as a Continuous Functional Unit
Breig described the pons-cord tissue tract, dura mater, and associated connective tissues as a continuous functional and biomechanical unit. His work clearly demonstrated that mechanical stress applied in one region is transmitted throughout the entire cord-dural complex—foreshadowing the Cranio-Spinal Meningeal Functional Unit (C-SMFU) foundational to TTC
.

3. Tension Can Be Both Global and Local
He differentiated between localized stress fields and global overstretching, emphasizing that pathological tension can arise from external vertebral distortions or internal lesions, and then transmit through the dural system to distant areas, inducing symptoms via altered afferent signaling
.

4. Axial Displacement and Tethering of the Spinal Cord
Breig showed that postural changes like flexion and extension alter tension in the spinal canal, causing displacement of the spinal cord and dura, a phenomenon now understood as relevant to global tone and stress adaptation mechanisms in tonal chiropractic
.

5. Clinical Insight into Tension-Induced Neurological Dysfunction
He noted that clinical symptoms could be explained not by gross lesion or compression, but by histodynamic tension (pathological mechanical stress on neural tissues), which disrupts normal conductivity and nervous system function
.

🧠 Application to Tonal Chiropractic:
These findings directly support the tonal model’s assertion that:

Aberrant tone in the NeuroSpinal system (C-SMFU) is the primary interference.

Interference is initiated by tension (mechanical stress) rather than direct compression.

Global neurological effects can originate from focal dural stress.

Dr. Marvin Talsky and the TTC model specifically cite Breig as a major influence in identifying the C-SMFU as the tone-setting system, with tone change as the initiating subluxation event, not a downstream consequence
.

Alf Breig, M.D., Ph.D.: A pioneering neurosurgeon and researcher, Dr. Alf Breig provided groundbreaking anatomical and physiological insights that laid the foundation for the tonal chiropractic model. In his 1978 publication Adverse Mechanical Tension in the Central Nervous System, Breig demonstrated that mechanical tension within the spinal cord and meninges—independent of vertebral compression—can lead to significant neurological dysfunction. He introduced the concept of the dura mater, spinal cord, and pons-cord tract as a continuous biomechanical unit, capable of transmitting stress globally through the central nervous system. This challenged compression-based models and reframed interference as a tension-based phenomenon.

Further expanding this paradigm in his 1989 work Skull Traction and Cervical Cord Injury, Breig explored how cervical cord tension could be mitigated through changes in head and neck positioning, and how relaxation of the spinal cord from a pathologically elongated state could reverse certain neurological deficits. He emphasized that recovery from dysfunction often involved reducing cord tension—validating the idea that the nervous system’s tone and mechanical state play a critical role in function and healing. These findings directly support the TTC view of the C-SMFU (NeuroSpinal System) as a continuous, contractile, and tension-sensitive system central to subluxation physiology.
(Breig, Adverse Mechanical Tension in the Central Nervous System, 1978; Skull Traction and Cervical Cord Injury, 1989)

.

Craniosacral Concepts (Don Cohen, D.C., 1995): In his 1995 teaching manual An Introduction to Craniosacral Therapy, chiropractor Don Cohen synthesized osteopathic insights from William Sutherland and John Upledger into a practical and accessible chiropractic framework. Cohen described the Craniosacral system—including the cranial membranes, dura mater, and cerebrospinal fluid—as a continuous, hydroelastic, and intelligent membrane network that transmits tension and stores unresolved stress patterns. He emphasized concepts such as the still point, spontaneous unwinding, and the ability of the dural system to store and release potential energy as part of the healing process. These ideas align with key tenets of tonal chiropractic, particularly in TTC, which also engages the body’s capacity for reorganization through minimal input, directional unwinding, and the facilitation of self-correction through the NeuroSpinal system. Cohen’s text framed craniosacral work not as passive technique, but as a “neurological conversation” with the body’s inner wisdom—setting an important philosophical precedent for modern tonal models.

(Cohen, An Introduction to Craniosacral Therapy: Anatomy, Function, and Treatment, 1995)

1. John F. Thie did not originate the core techniques of muscle testing or Applied Kinesiology.

While ICAK developed as a clinical diagnostic tool, TFH was deliberately non-diagnostic, non-therapeutic, and non-medical in practice, appealing to a broader community including laypeople, educators, and holistic practitioners
.

Yes—Hermann von Helmholtz’s work in On the Sensations of Tone (first published in German in 1863 as Die Lehre von den Tonempfindungen) is indeed supportive of the tonal chiropractic paradigm—particularly in how tone, frequency, and resonance relate to human physiology.

Key Contributions Relevant to Tonal Chiropractic:
Tone as Foundational to Perception: Helmholtz established that sensory experience—especially auditory perception—is based on the resonance and frequency response of specific anatomical structures (e.g. the basilar membrane in the cochlea). This set the scientific stage for understanding the body as an instrument that detects, responds to, and organizes itself around vibrational input.

Resonance and Harmonic Frequency: Helmholtz introduced the idea that complex vibrational input is decomposed into component frequencies via mechanical resonance in the sensory system. This principle of harmonic decomposition underpins many tonal chiropractic ideas, particularly around harmonic resonance and the vibrational tuning of the spine or NeuroSpinal system.

Physiological Basis for Vibrational Interaction: He was one of the first to experimentally validate that physiological systems operate based on vibratory principles, not just chemical or structural inputs—this matches the conceptual underpinnings of tonal chiropractic, which emphasizes tone and frequency as primary organizing factors.

Scientific Legitimization of Tone in Biology: His blending of physics and physiology helped legitimize the exploration of tone, frequency, and vibration as meaningful inputs for both function and dysfunction—helping bridge the gap between classical science and vitalistic frameworks.

Suggested Bullet for Section 2.4a:
Hermann von Helmholtz, Ph.D., M.D. (1863): In On the Sensations of Tone, Helmholtz introduced foundational concepts of resonance, harmonic frequency, and vibrational perception in the human body. His research showed that biological systems, particularly auditory structures, are intrinsically tuned to vibratory frequencies and organize sensory experience through mechanical resonance. Though rooted in auditory physiology, his discoveries laid the groundwork for understanding the human body as a vibrationally responsive system—supporting the tonal chiropractic paradigm’s emphasis on tone, frequency, and resonance as essential organizing principles in health and adaptability.
(Helmholtz, On the Sensations of Tone, 1863)

Applied Kinesiology (AK): Introduced by Dr. George Goodheart in 1964, Applied Kinesiology (AK) brought manual muscle testing into chiropractic as a diagnostic and therapeutic tool. While not a tonal technique per se, AK contributed significantly to the evolution of tonal analysis by establishing muscle testing as a method of engaging in a “neurological conversation” with the body—similar in principle to the use of pressure testing and leg checks.  This broader concept of “asking the body” aligns with the nonlinear assessment strategies of tonal chiropractic, in contrast to the static, linear logic of motion palpation. Applied Kinesiology (AK): Introduced by Dr. George Goodheart in 1964, Applied Kinesiology (AK) brought manual muscle testing into chiropractic as a diagnostic and therapeutic tool. While not a tonal technique per se, AK contributed significantly to the evolution of tonal analysis by establishing muscle testing as a form of “neurological conversation” with the body—similar in principle to the use of pressure testing and leg checks.

This broader concept of “asking the body” aligns with the nonlinear assessment strategies of tonal chiropractic, contrasting with the static, linear logic of motion palpation. Since its introduction, many chiropractic approaches—across Osseous, OsseoTonal, and Tonal models—have evolved the application of this neurological conversation, reinforcing the idea that the nervous system can be queried directly for real-time biofeedback.

On an iPhone using the Pages app, there’s no direct “Paste and Match Style” like on a Mac. But here are a few workarounds to help you keep formatting consistent:

Option 1: Paste, Then Apply Style Manually
Paste normally into your Pages document (tap and hold > Paste).

Tap the pasted text, then tap the paintbrush icon (Format) at the top.

Choose the appropriate paragraph style, font, size, and spacing to match your body or heading text.

Option 2: Use Style Copying
Tap and hold on some existing text in your document that has the style you want.

Tap “Copy Style” from the pop-up menu.

Then tap and hold on your newly pasted text.

Tap “Paste Style.”

This will apply the exact same formatting.

Option 3: Use a Placeholder
First, duplicate an existing paragraph in the style you want.

Then replace the text inside that paragraph with the new text you're pasting.

CranioSacral Therapy: Developed in the 1970s by Dr. John Upledger, CranioSacral Therapy (CST) built upon the foundational insights of Dr. William Sutherland, who in the 1930s identified the subtle rhythmic motion of cranial bones and the role of the dural system in systemic health. Upledger expanded this into a clinical model at Michigan State University, describing the craniosacral system as a continuous membrane extending from cranium to sacrum that influences neural coherence, emotional integration, and physiological adaptability. CST emphasized minimal input and innate self-correction—principles that resonate with and support the tonal chiropractic model. Don Cohen later contributed to the system’s accessibility through educational texts in the 1990s, helping codify its relevance to tonal analysis. (Sutherland, 1939; Upledger & Vredevoogd, 1983; Cohen, 1995)
